1. MONGOLIA'S URBAN SERVICES SECTOR
A. MONGOLIA: A COUNTRY IN ECONOMIC TRANSITION
1.1   Mongolia, landlocked between Russia and China, is a relatively poor country,
with a per capita gross domestic product (GDP) of only about $440 in 1996. Compared
with many other low-income countries, Mongolia's economy is relatively well
diversified, with agriculture-primarily livestock-accounting for about 20 percent of
GDP; industry-principally mining and wool, cashmere, leather and food processing-
for 45 percent of GDP; and the services sectors, including a large government sector, a
weak banking system and a growing private wholesale and retail trading sector,
accounting for the balance.
1.2   Mongolia is among the world's most sparsely-populated countries (1.46 per km2),
with an area of some 1.6 million km2-half the size of India-and a population of only
2.32 million people. About a quarter of the population are nomadic herders whose
livestock outnumber the human population 11 to 1. Land, although relatively abundant,
has limited productivity and a short growing season because of the country's harsh,
semiarid continental climate and relatively high altitude. Besides sizable reserves of
copper and other minerals, Mongolia has a potential for petroleum production in
commercial quantities. The population is well educated, with an adult literacy rate of 96
percent and a sizable skilled labor force. Until 1990 and the end of its 70 years as a
centrally planned economy, Mongolia benefited from substantial grant financial
assistance (estimated at 30 percent of GDP annually) and managerial assistance from the
former Soviet Union (FSU).
1.3   With the collapse of the Soviet Union, the attendant sudden withdrawal of its aid,
and the collapse of demand from traditional Soviet-bloc trading partners who accounted
for 95 percent of its exports, Mongolia suffered a massive economic shock. Real GDP
fell by 20 percent during 1990-93, causing a sharp deterioration in both the external
payment position and the Government's fiscal position. The Government responded with
an austerity program characterized by gradual liberalization toward a market economy,
including price decontrol and privatization. In 1992-93, small enterprises and livestock
ownership were rapidly privatized, and by end-1994, the assets of about 1,200 large
public enterprises were privatized, although the remaining 200 public enterprises still to
be reformed account for over 30 percent of employment and value added in the economy.
1.4   At the same time, hyperinflation forced severe government cutbacks, causing
delivery of social services to plummet and with them, social conditions:  school
attendance dropped 30 percent, maternal mortality doubled, the real value of pensions fell
by 60 percent, wages nearly halved, and an estimated 30 percent of the population still



- 2 -
live below the poverty line. During this period, the Association assisted the Government
with foreign exchange support for critical imports, technical assistance in key
macroeconomic and sectoral areas and investments and technical assistance to begin
rehabilitating the transportation system. In 1996, a poverty assessment study (Report No.
15723-MOG) was backed up by projects to alleviate the effects of the economic
transition on the poor or vulnerable, and to rehabilitate a major coal mine.
1.5   By the end of 1996, the economy appeared to have weathered the worst of the
shocks, but is still fragile. After suffering negative growth during 1991-93, GDP grew by
2.5 percent in 1994 and 6.3 percent in 1995. But with a 33 percent decline in copper
prices and 10 percent drop in cashmere export prices, growth in the first half of 1996 fell
to less than 1 percent. Although major macroeconomic targets agreed with external
lenders are being met, the pace of reform slowed in 1995-96, and the challenges for the
policymakers remain daunting. Mongolia's leaders must: continue and deepen structural
reform; maintain satisfactory levels of income; reduce the poverty level; improve the
welfare of pensioners; and reduce inflation. Though down from its 1993 high of about
325 percent, inflation was still about 50 to 60 percent in 1995/96, primarily due to loose
monetary and fiscal policies, excessive bank and government credit creation, weak budget
revenue generation and defaults on debt payments by state enterprises. Two recently
approved IDA projects aimed at banking and enterprise reform should help with some of
these problems.
1.6   Mongolia's new national government, elected in mid-1996, has announced a
wide-ranging, accelerated reform agenda. The major objectives are to: (a) speed up
company privatization; (b) reduce tax burdens on producers by 30 percent; (c) improve
tax administration and broaden the tax base; (d) promote foreign investment; (e) improve
foreign assistance coordination; (f) refocus social security on the most needy; (g) reduce
govermnent expenditure and, to achieve better governance, prepare a code of ethics for all
government members.
1.7   By end-1996, the Government had already implemented some courageous sectoral
and macroeconomic reforms conforming with the goals outlined above. These included:
(a) raising electricity and heating tariffs by 60 percent; coal prices by 45 percent and
petroleum prices by 29 to 60 percent; (b) abolishing price commissions that were
controlling prices of basic commodities in major urban centers; (c) enacting new laws on
central and commercial banking operations; and (d) reducing the number of ministries
and departments from 16 to 9.
B. THE URBAN SETTING
Urbanization in Mongolia
1.8   Of the national population of 2.32 million in 1995, about 55 percent were urban.
Overall population growth in the 1990s has been about 2 percent a year, while urban
growth averaged only 1 percent but was concentrated in the three largest cities, as smaller
rural centers declined. Political and economic changes in the 1990s are believed to have



- 3 -
caused increased migration to the larger urban areas at the expense of the smaller towns.
Currently, about a quarter of the population or an estimated 620,000 people lives in the
capital city of Ulaanbaatar (UB), and another 25 percent lives in other urban centers,
including the three next largest cities-Darhan with 91,300, Erdenet with 62,900 and
Choibalsan with 46,400. Except for those living in the capitals of the country's 17
aimags or provinces, averaging 20,300 each, most other people live in widely dispersed,
and much smaller, urban centers, essentially large villages.
Urban Services Delivery
1.9    Mongolia's cities bear the imprint of socialist urban planning, that is, a reliance on
rigid physical norms with little regard to user demand, technological alternatives, or
realistic resource constraints. The upshot of these policies has been lopsided urban
development. The combination of high standards for publicly-financed formal housing
and urban services and inadequate resources to provide such housing and services to all
city residents stranded a large segment of the urban population in self-financed housing in
plotted developments, where standards of facilities and services are much lower than
those in the formal housing areas. The traditional housing areas, known as ger areas,
comprise individually fenced plots and clusters of traditional round tents (gers)1 and/or
assorted low-cost structures ranging from wooden shacks to a scattering of two-story
brick houses.
1.10   Ger Area Services. Due to their supposedly temporary nature, the ger areas have
received the bare minimum of infrastructure investment, usually only individual
electricity connections and communal water collection points. The ger areas lack other
basic services, such as paved, or at least stabilized access roads, community services,
piped water supply and sanitation. Pit latrines are universal, and coal, wood or dung-fired
stoves are used for heating and cooking. There are generally few communal social
facilities. Local governments lay out and allot the individual plots to residents, with an
occupation right of unspecified duration. Whereas industries and apartments generally lie
within the serviced areas, most ger housing areas fall outside the piped water and
sewerage systems. Instead, ger residents must rely on water expensively trucked to and
sold from public water kiosks. And, while apartment residents obtain almost all their
services from public entities at highly subsidized prices, most services in the ger are
charged at full cost. This continues to hold true even though the ger areas house the
largest part of the urban poor, who constitute about 46 percent of the ger population. The
average income of ger households varies by location, but is estimated to be about $65
equivalent, or about 70 percent of average household income in the formal serviced areas.
1.11  In recent years, ger areas have tended to develop without control, because
newcomers generally identify a plot first and only afterward regularize occupancy with
I Ger tents have light, demountable wooden frame construction covered by several layers of felt and
canvas.



- 4 -
the local government administration. This results in occupancy of land unsuited for
residential use and difficult or expensive to provide with infrastructure or services. Such
is the case with gers situated on steep slopes, near hilltops above the reach of water
supply reservoirs, and in areas subject to flooding. In the future, the local authorities will
need to enforce controls on new settlement and clearly indicate the land open to new
settlement. The low density of the ger areas (at 10 plots/hectare-ha) and their spread
over an increasing area cannot continue as this also makes delivery of urban services
difficult and expensive. In this regard, the proposed project would include a study of ger
development planning in UB.
1.12  Urban Services in the Formal Housing Sector. With the important exception of
the ger areas, Mongolia has a reasonable-but possibly unaffordable-level of urban
services, mostly built over the past 20 years.
*  Over 100 piped water systems are operated in all centers, generally from deep
wells. These systems, augmented by tankered water outside the distribution
systems, serve about 81 percent of the urban population.
*  Major cities, two provincial centers and large towns are served by sewerage
systems and associated sewage treatment. Water and sewerage and other
services are handled by the same unit-whether a municipal company in cities
or a municipal department in large towns.
* Solid waste management is handled by the local governments. All urban
centers are served with some type of solid waste collection system.
1.13  Service Quality. The quality of the urban services provided has deteriorated
badly, in part because of the economic retrenchment and deep cuts in government
spending for maintenance. Since 1990, most capital investment and expansion projects
have been halted, and even minor repairs of existing assets has been severely curtailed.
In general, the formal utility services face severe equipment problems. All equipment
originated from FSU countries and is now difficult to replace or maintain with spares
because of the severance of relationships with previous suppliers and inadequate hard
currency to pay for imports. For instance, more than 10 percent of the wells in the well
fields supplying UB are inoperative due to faulty pumps; the municipal sewage plant
treats only 75 percent of the sewage due to defective pumps and equipment; and pipe
breaks take three to four days to repair due to lack of materials and equipment.
1.14  Low levels of cost recovery, typical of the Soviet era when users contributed little
and utilities received substantial subsidies for new investments, have exacerbated service
delivery problems. Metering-whether of water, heat, or power-on apartment blocks or
individual premises is almost nonexistent. Water and sewerage are billed on the basis of
low assumed consumption norms, and collection of bills may be as low as 50 percent in
the aimags and 80 percent in UB. While tariff levels cover full costs of the present low
level of operations and depreciation, they are insufficient to cover the costs of adequate



- D -
levels of maintenance and rehabilitation, much less new investments in the systems.
Recovery of solid wastes service costs is limited and has led to deteriorating and
unsatisfactory collection and disposal services. Solid waste is disposed to sites operated
as open dumps, accepting mixed industrial, institutional and domestic wastes, and having
no environmental controls. The lack of funds has also affected the staff working in the
sector, who have not benefited from training in efficient operational procedures.
1.15  The lack of maintenance and the overall deterioration in standards have also had
environmental repercussions. Industrial sewage is being discharged directly to rivers, and
aquifers used for cities' water supply are beginning to show pollution from heavy metals
and high concentrations of chemical oxygen demand. The Government's (draft) National
Environmental Action Plan places high priority on addressing these problems. Unless
urgent remedial steps are taken, the cost of restoring reliable urban services and a
sustainable environment is likely to rise sharply.
C. SECTOR INSTITUTIONS
Ministry of Infrastructure Development
1.16  The Ministry of Infrastructure Development (MID) has been responsible for
public services policy since 1994. MID has broad responsibilities, ranging from tourism
to coal mining and including the Department of Urban Development, Housing and Public
Utilities. The latter is responsible for: (a) formulating urban development policies;
(b) preparing the legal framework for urban development, housing, public services;
management coordination and inspection; (c) planning the funding of public services, and
(d) managing design and research and provision of government expertise. Reflecting the
importance now being given to municipal services by Mongolia's new National
government, in January 1997 a formerly semiautonomous government company, the
State Concern for Municipal Utilities, was absorbed in MID and renamed the Urban
Services Agency. Public utility companies in the provincial (aimag) centers and the
water supply and sewerage company in UB have to date been only nominally coordinated
by the Ministry; real responsibility rested with the local municipalities and aimag
governors.
D. GOVERNMENT SECTOR STRATEGY
1.17  After little action on the urban front since 1991 due to the economic crisis, the
Government's sector policies are being reinvigorated with a market orientation. In
December 1996, Parliament approved important Directives for the Economic and Social
Development of Mongolia, providing for (a) the drafting of a law on a real estate tax,
(b) implementation of land ownership measures and procedures, (c) an inventory and
evaluation of state-owned fixed assets, including property, (d) development of a housing
program, with new standards, and the resolution of problems relating to the financing of
private housing, and (e) renovation of water supply and sewerage facilities in cities and
settlements and improvement in their maintenance and levels of service. A Government
plan also exists to concentrate settlement and national infrastructure development around



five regional growth poles (including UB), supported by transport, communications and
energy corridors.
1.18  The new development policy is consistent with a draft statement of Mongolia's
urban development policy in its Medium-Term Strategy. The Strategy recognizes the
need for: (a) private sector and individual initiatives, not the Government, in replacing
ger housing; (b) better public provision of water supply and sanitation to the urban
population to improve productivity and quality of life and to reduce poverty; and (c) cost
recovery for the infrastructure services provided. The policy action matrix included in
the Strategy, similar to some of the goals of the proposed IDA project, would: (a) provide
water supply services to ger areas around UB; (b) establish effective demand
management (pricing) mechanisms that reflect the scarcity value of water resources; and
(c) improve sector planning in peri-urban areas.  While resource limitations-both
financial and human-may temper the implementation of the Strategy, the Government
seems prepared to expand the reforms already introduced in other areas of the economy to
the water supply sector. To this end, it is exploring ways to enhance its operational and
financial efficiencies while mitigating the inequality between ger areas and the apartment
sector. The proposed project would support both goals.
E. RATIONALE FOR IDA INVOLVEMENT IN THE PROPOSED PROJECT
1.19  The proposed project supports three major goals of the Bank Group's June 1995
Country Assistance Strategy (CAS), i.e., to protect the poor and other groups vulnerable
to the effects of the country's economic transition, improve economic infrastructure, and
promote a commercial orientation in local public enterprises. By delivering water supply
and other urban services to predominantly poor ger areas in UB, the project supports both
the Bank Group's objective of poverty alleviation in the country, and the Government's
own Poverty Alleviation Program launched in 1994 to reduce the proportion of the
population living below the poverty line and ease the social burden of structural reforms
introduced during the transition to a market economy. By improving UB's water
distribution network to benefit ger, apartment and other areas, the project responds to
immediate urban infrastructure requirements that have gone unmet due to severe funding
shortages. Project assistance for development and corporatization of UB's water supply
and sewerage utility is expected to strengthen the economic and financial management of
the system and facilitate the utility's development as a fully commercial entity. The
project would also introduce market-oriented provisions for, and management of, urban
services by providing better cost-recovery mechanisms as well as financial incentives for
water conservation and operational efficiency.
1.20 The project directly supports the Government's recently announced sector
strategy (para. 1.17) and complements other IDA-funded initiatives in Mongolia as well
as those financed by other donors. It builds on recommendations of IDA's 1994 informal
sector review, carried out as part of project identification, findings of the 1996 Mongolia
Poverty Assessment (Report No. 15723-MOG) and the Poverty Alleviation for
Vulnerable Groups Project (Cr. 2760-MN) now assisting the unemployed, disabled and



- 7-
poor women and children; the success for the ongoing implementation of this latter
project critically depends on the association of the Poverty Program Office with the local
Group Associations. In its goal of reducing water losses in UB, it complements a
proposed similar operation in the power sector to reduce energy losses in the capital.
Project initiatives also share goals of similar work being done by other multi- and
bilateral agencies, particularly the United Nations Development Programme (UNDP),
Asian Development Bank (ADB) and the Japan International Cooperation Agency
(JICA). All three are funding small water supply, sanitation and ger area improvement
schemes in other areas of the country, and JICA is supporting UB's rehabilitation of
water supply facilities; it also supports preparation of a drainage master plan for UB and,
in future, is expected to finance priority works under the plan.
F. LESSONS LEARNED FROM PREVIOUS PROJECTS
1.21  The Association has assisted Mongolia with three projects providing balance of
payments or technical assistance support, two for structural adjustment of the banking
and enterprise sectors, two for rehabilitation of the transport system and of the Baganuur
coal mine near UB, and one for protection of vulnerable groups during this difficult
period. The principal lesson leamed from these operations is the lack of local familiarity
with basic project activities like procurement and disbursement, which can result in
significant project startup delays and slower-than-expected progress thereafter. AusAID-
funded assistance for preparation of the project, therefore, included preparation of major
procurement packages for the first two project years.  A  four-member project
management team of intemational consultants would also assist the Project Management
Unit during the first 18 to 24 months of implementation. Such assistance should
accelerate project startup and build local capabilities. In addition, counterpart funding
constraints experienced under the Transport Rehabilitation Project (Cr. 2615, approved in
May 1994), due to the imposition during implementation of import duties and sales tax
on project inputs, would be avoided by including these taxes in the project cost estimate
and budgetary planning by the Central and Municipal Govermnent to provide the full
local funding requirement.
1.22  Experience gained outside of Mongolia has also influenced Project design. In a
review of urban projects in the past 20 years, the Operations Evaluation Department's
report "Twenty Years of Lending for Urban Development" stresses the importance of:
(a) ensuring ownership of the project through participation of beneficiaries from the early
stages to improve project design and reveal effective demand; (b) enhancing service
delivery efficiency in a sustainable way through cost recovery, based on effective
demand; and (c) making more efficient use of the existing infrastructure before
developing additional networks, as part of the borrower's clear understanding of
investment priorities.
1.23  The proposed project incorporates these lessons.  First, to ensure project
ownership, ger area leaders and representatives discussed their needs with the project
preparation team during several stages of the preparation process. And, to prepare



- 8 -
stakeholders to assume responsibility for management and financial reforms under the
project's institutional development component, during project preparation key utility
managers and staff participated in a study tour of major utilities being successfully
operated on commercial principles in neighboring countries. The positive feedback from
the latter exercise should strengthen commitments to the project's reform agenda.
Second, efficiency gains and cost recovery are key aims of the project's institutional and
financial development activities. Third, the project is posited on maximizing the use of
existing facilities rather than constructing new ones. Proposed water supply system
investments would utilize existing underused reservoirs to reduce water supply costs,
while the project's provision of some 13,000 water meters would enhance water
conservation, reduce water supply costs, and alleviate the need for new system expansion
during the next five years. Finally, project improvements in the ger areas will have
accompanying environmental benefits due to improved sanitation and solid waste
collection facilities.



- 9 -
2. URBAN SERVICES IN ULAANBAATAR
A. ULAANBAATAR: How IT HAS DEVELOPED
2.1   Ulaanbaatar, with about 620,000 people in 1995 or 27 percent of the national
population, is Mongolia's capital and the country's foremost city, having 42 percent of
commercial activity, 50 percent of housing and 52 percent of investment activity. Since
about 1970, its population has doubled, having grown at an annual average 6.8 percent in
the 1970s, at more modest rates in the 1980s, and at about 2 percent in the 1990s. UB
comprises six contiguous districts (duureg) and two districts enclaved in the adjoining
province-the mining town of Baganuur about 100 km away and a former military base
south of the city. While UB officially encompasses a total area of some 354,300 ha, the
built-up areas account for only 20,000 ha. The site of the present city, lying between the
Tuul River flood plain and the Khentii mountains, has been inhabited since ancient times.
2.2  In the 1950s, the Government promoted development of the industrial power
stations in the UB region, demolished much of the traditional settlements in the central
city area and erected apartment blocks in their place to house the inhabitants, together
with a skilled work force from Russia. The city plan prepared at that time, and its
subsequent modifications, envisaged the construction of a modem city of functionally
distinct zones, large-scale infrastructure and rented housing of four- to eight-story
apartment blocks. From the 1950s through the 1980s, this plan was substantially
implemented using the Government's control of production, construction, employment
and population movement. However, since the democratic reforms, these controls have
been loosened, costs and prices have risen, but unemployment has sharply increased and
for most people, real income has dropped. Due to these factors and to privatization of
industries, Government revenues have declined, as has its ability to limit in-migration or
maintain the pace of infrastructure construction to accommodate the rising growth of
population and small-scale enterprises.
2.3   Due to these economic and political changes, apartment building has now almost
stopped, and the majority of urban growth is in the ger areas surrounding the core
"apartment city." The future of Ulaanbaatar seems to be in "ger development," gers
being, in effect, low-cost suburban settlements. Families wishing to obtain a plot apply
to the local khoroo (the smallest level of the city administration) and are allocated a
300 m  or 500 m2 lot located and staked out by a survey from the city. Most new
residents obtain land in this way, although there is an increasingly active real estate
market. Only 10 percent of the plots are occupied by unregistered squatters. The
government-owned land and the region's semidesert, uncultivated landscape allow rapid
expansion of ger development with few legal or natural constraints.



- 10-
2.4   The individual plots are laid out in simple rectangular blocks with wide public
rights-of-way between plots. The plot-holders construct wooden fences around their
plots, then erect the ger tent, possibly a conventional wood frame permanent building,
and a pit privy toilet. Between one and four families may live on this plot, and one or
more of the families may periodically move to herd livestock in rural areas, then return to
their urban ger. Many families keep livestock on their plots or on the hills nearby.
Livestock forms a significant part of household incomes, especially since the reduction of
secure formal employment.
2.5   Socioeconomic surveys of ger residents in UB indicate that, although household
incomes are low (median income is $76 month for an average size household of 4.8),
income distribution is still fairly even (a positive legacy from the Communist period),
although it is gradually becoming more skewed as the market economy takes hold.
2.6   Despite the economic problems, UB has a number of advantages over other
capital cities in the developing world. It is not yet too large to be unmanageable. There
is no widespread squatting, and the settlement process is fairly orderly. Settlement
densities are fairly low, allowing scope to introduce services, and ger plot sites are large
enough to permit incremental housing development and for residents to obtain a valuable
future real estate asset. Distribution of income and assets (including urban land) is, as
yet, fairly equal, and social divisions (regional, ethnic, religious) are insignificant.
Literacy and education levels are high, potentially allowing informed dialogue and
participation in issues of urban planning, management and development.
B. URBAN SERVICE PROVISION IN MODERN AND TRADITIONAL HOUSING AREAS
2.7   The Modern Sector. About 55 percent of UB's population lives in fully-serviced
public housing estates, comprised of 3- to 12-story apartment buildings in prefabricated
modular construction. The housing estates, clustered near the city center and along the
city's main roads, are relatively well-served by public transport. The apartments have
cold and hot water, toilets and bathrooms, conventional piped sewage collection, on-site
and off-site piped stormwater drainage, electricity, water heating, elevators in higher
buildings and solid waste collection from apartment chutes. USAG, the municipal water
supply and sewerage company, provides cold water to the housing sites; and hot water is
generated in community heating centers on the housing sites. Apartment rents and utility
charges have until recently been heavily subsidized.
2.8   Ger Areas. The remainder of the population or some 250,000 to 270,000 people
live in 18 ger areas that sprawl mainly over the outlying hills and valleys to the north,
northwest and northeast of UB, several kilometers from the city's economic activity.
Densities in these areas are low, primarily due to the large plot sizes and the sporadic
distribution of ger settlements. At these low densities, the cost of servicing ger areas is
high, especially for roads and water supply. While the proposed project would extend
piped water supply to public water kiosks in ger areas, individual household connections
are currently unaffordable to most families. A greater problem exists when ger plots are



- 11 -
extended higher than the existing water reservoirs (the highest being 1,470 m). Above
this level, the current pressure in the water system cannot service kiosks in the areas.
2.9   The higher cost of providing municipal services to the ger areas does not explain
the minimal level of services there, which contrast sharply with the European-style,
middle-income standard of the apartments. Ger services are generally as follows:
*  excreta are generally disposed to pit latrines, and sullage to the ground and
natural drainage channels;
*  access is via rough, poorly-bridged earth roads. The wide rights-of-way are
often eroded by stormwater flows;
*  there is virtually no formal stormwater drainage system;
*  solid waste is sporadically collected from bins at the end of residential lanes
by local municipal workers;
-  high heating costs, in individual private wood or coal stoves, can consume
about 10 percent of average household expenditure;
-  housing is solely by private household investment and, to be affordable to low
ger incomes, is mostly in poorly-insulated wooden shacks or the traditional
nomadic tent-the ger;
-  water is supplied from neighborhood kiosks filled by public tanker trucks;
3  some schools have been built but virtually no community centers, health
clinics, or recreation areas. There are few shops or consumer markets, and the
latter are unsanitary and create local traffic congestion;
*  private transport is the dominant mode due to infrequent and low-density
public bus service; and
*  the failed privatization of ger bath houses nearly eliminated washing facilities
in the ger (currently only a few of the original 20 bathhouses are operating).
2.10  The inadequacy of public services to the ger is reflected in significant health
differences between ger and apartment dwellers. A 1993 UNICEF nutrition survey found
that infants born into ger area families had a 60 percent higher risk of low weight than
those born in apartments. Further, the incidence of diarrhea and acute respiratory
infections in ger children under five is about double the rate of children in apartments. A
ger household survey carried out during preparation of the proposed project indicates an
average household size of five people, with a median income of about Tug 53,200 a
month (about $76 at the current exchange rate), more than half of which is spent for food.
An estimated 30 percent of Ulaanbaatar's ger population live below the poverty line.



- 12-
C. WATER SUPPLY AND SANITATION
The Network and Operations
2.11  Water Supply and Distribution. Most of UB's raw water supply is from four
groundwater extraction wellfields on an aquifer near the south-central part of the city at
an altitude of about 1,100 m. Streams from adjacent hills maintain the aquifer, thus
development, especially highly polluting land uses such as solid waste dumps, industry,
etc., needs to be located away from  these areas and adjacent slopes.  About
160,000 m3/day of water is supplied. Tests indicate that water quality in the sources is
good, and water pumped from the wells is only lightly chlorinated at the well heads.
From there, the water is pumped through distribution pipelines to industry, some
government buildings, to a water trucking operation delivering to ger water kiosks, and to
Central Heating Points (CTPs) that deliver hot-water heating as well as hot and cold
running water to apartments. A Japanese grant under discussion (para. 1.20) will provide
some equipment and meters in 1997 to assist in the monitoring of CTPs.
2.12  About 55 percent of the population, mostly in apartment blocks, is served by a
piped water supply. The distribution network consists of about 300 km of unlined steel
or cast iron pipes, ranging from 150 to 700 mm in diameter. Pipe trench depths are
generally between 2 and 3 m, in order to avoid freezing in UB's cold winters when
average daily temperatures range from -13 to -25°C. The currently inadequate service,
with intermittent flows and pressures, would be improved under the proposed project.
Details of the UB water supply system are given in Annex 1.
2.13  Reservoirs. The existing reservoir system is both underutilized (by half of its
capacity) and unintegrated, to the detriment of water pressure in the system as well as
service coverage. With three commissioned reservoirs (the Northwest, the "3/4 district"
and the Tasgan) in the hills north and east of UB not linked to each other, and two
completed reservoirs (the Northwest and the "X") not yet commissioned, system capacity
is far from being fully utilized to provide adequate system storage or gravity system
pressure. And none of the reservoirs, except the easternmost, are high enough to service
by gravity the large ger population in a large valley north of UB. For the most part,
pressure is maintained in the network by direct pumping from the well-head sources.
However, many areas suffer from inadequate water supply and pressure, and facilities
serving high-rise apartment blocks are not equipped to boost-pump water to the higher
floors in nine-story buildings. The proposed project would increase system efficiency
and improve pressure and service by bringing the unused reservoirs into operation and
constructing a transmission main to link four reservoirs to a new section of the city
supply network, also to be constructed under the project. Additional reservoirs would
also be constructed to provide a piped water supply to local ger communities.
2.14 Water Demand. The inequity of water supply in UB is indicated by the system's
skewed demand figures, which show about 55 percent of the population receiving about
75 percent of the piped water supply and 45 percent of the population (in ger) receiving a



- 13 -
mere 1.3 percent of the total. In 1995, water demand in UB totaled about 161,935 m3/d,
of which 121,532 m3/d was from apartments (296,420 persons at 410 lcd), 38,159 m3/d
from industry, and 2,244 m3/d from ger residents (224,443 persons at 10 lcd). While
UB's 74,000 apartments with hot and cold running water housed about 323,000 in 1995,
not all were supplied from the municipal network. Projections of future demand in the
year 2010 assume an average annual population growth of about 2.25 percent for
apartments dwellers, an apartment population of about 415,000 persons, and water
consumption at 320 lcd to reflect an increase in the standard of living and use of
appliances such as family washing machines. The ger population is expected to expand
at about an annual average 2.25 percent, reaching some 313, 800 in 2010 and consuming
about 25 lcd by that time. On this basis, water supply demand in 2010 would total some
217,600 m3/d, 7,840 m3/d from the ger, 132,630 m3/d from apartments and 77,137 m3/d
from nonresidential and industrial users.
2.15  Wastage. Studies indicate that about 30 percent of the 410 lcd (125 lcd) supplied
to apartments is wasted in water losses from leaking plumbing fixtures in apartments.
Since residential consumers' charges are based on norms rather than actual metered
consumption and USAG's low flat charges absorb only a small percentage of household
income, there are no incentives in the current system to conserve water. USAG receives
no revenue for an estimated 50 percent of the water it produces.
2.16  Ger Water Supply. Water to the ger areas is supplied by trucks drawing mostly
from USAG water stations, and service is unreliable, inconsistent and expensive, to both
the city and the residents. Ger dwellers are forced to get supplementary water from
elsewhere-rivers, surface water sources that can be polluted, wells or rainwater. The
tanker service is operated by TANK, a separate USAG department. The trucked-in water
is stored in tanks at 262 coal-stove-heated public water kiosks in the ger neighborhoods,
which are supposed to operate for about eight hours a day. Access to some kiosks is very
difficult and in adverse weather conditions tankers fail to supply the kiosks, forcing them
to close and leaving the ger residents unserved. A shortage or lack of heating fuel,
freezing or the absence of an attendant can also close the kiosks. The attendants,
employed by TANK, collect tokens from ger purchasers for a specific volume of water,
which the purchasers draw from a pipe at the kiosk. USAG/TANK charge the full cost of
tankered water to the ger. For water drawn from kiosks the price is Tug 400/'000 liter (in
1997)-about 20 times the Tug 20/'000 liter price paid by apartment residents in 1995
and 12 times the Tug 34/'000 liter tariff introduced in January 1997.
2.17  Water is usually fetched by women and children over a distance of 200 to 500 m,
in hand carts from kiosks to homes, over mostly rough, hilly, earth paths and roads. In
the winter, ice and snow impede access to the kiosks by both tanker trucks and ger
residents and water supply is erratic. Ger consumption from kiosks is about 7.5 to
10.0 lcd; the amount is limited by both cost and the difficulty of transporting the water
and is well below the minimum requirement for good health and sanitation, generally
considered to be 20 lcd. The total water supplied by TANK to ger areas, although



- 14-
probably underestimated by USAG, is only 2,244 m3/day, not even 2 percent of USAG's
daily demand.
2.18  Sewerage.  UB's apartment housing estates and institutional premises are
connected to a conventional 80 km underground sewer system operated by USAG. Most
industrial wastewater is discharged to the public sewerage system. The discharges are
expected to meet certain standards, but the latter are not rigorously enforced. An
industrial wastewater pretreatment plant of a 20,000 m3/day capacity exists but the plant
is old and not operating fully nor efficiently. It serves the wool and leather industries but
due to poor performance, outflow from the plant and into the domestic sewerage system
is likely to exceed standards.  The data maintained measure few of the relevant
parameters but still show an effluent quality inconsistent with discharge standards to
public sewers (particularly for the one heavy metal measured). If industrial wastewater
does not satisfy discharge standards, the Municipality has powers to close the operation,
require three times normal sewerage charges and impose other sanctions. This is rarely
done, however.
2.19  The entire city sewerage system flows by gravity to a single sewage treatment
plant (STP) for domestic and industrial wastewater streams located 11 km west of the city
center. The STP was designed to treat 230,000 m 3/day, and actual flows are in the range
of 150,000 m3/day. Only a proportion (perhaps 80 percent) of water consumed is likely
to reach the sewerage system, at least 60,000 m3/day is from industrial wastewater and
infiltration. The current sewage flow is very close to the amount of USAG water
produced, and confirms that system losses are recovered in the sewers.
2.20  The STP provides primary, secondary and tertiary treatment stages. Primary and
physical treatment components of the plant were constructed in 1963 and 1968, with the
biological treatment [diffused air activated sludge plant (ASP)] added in 1979. There are
also tertiary treatment ponds and chlorination facilities, although these are not
operational. In addition, problems are being experienced with some of the equipment.
2.21  Treated wastewater flows from the main treatment plant are discharged to the
Tuul River south of UB's developed area. Although still satisfactory, there has been a
noticeable downward trend in effluent quality. The plant experiences problems due to the
absence of standby generation; in the event of power failure the entire raw domestic and
industrial wastewater flows are diverted directly to the river. This also occurs when
pump breakdown causes the flow rate to exceed operational pumping capacity. Operation
control is made more difficult by the absence of operating flow meters. There is a need to
rehabilitate most of the electromechanical equipment and the primary system of
degritting and desilting and IDA is assisting the Government to identify appropriate
advice and financial support for those investments.
2.22  Sewer charges in 1997 are Tug 34/'000 liters (Tug 20 1996) for apartment
dwellers and Tug 97/'000 liters (Tug 50 in 1996) for industrial, institutional and other



- 15 -
major generators, at 100 percent of the normative volume charged for water. The charges
are added to customers' water bills issued by USAG on the kantors.
2.23  With the lack of investment over recent years, the sewerage system, like the water
supply system, is deteriorating. In particular, there is an urgent need for rehabilitation of
critical lengths of sewer and key items of equipment, particularly pumps.
D. SOLID WASTE SERVICES
2.24  Solid waste collection and disposal are responsibilities of the Municipality of
Ulaanbaatar (MUB), which devolves collections to its six duureg (Districts) and disposal
to the Ulaanbaatar Waste Disposal Company (NUUTS), a wholly municipal-owned
company. Each duureg has established a General Maintenance Company (GMC)
responsible for refuse collection, street cleaning, park and green area maintenance and
development, and maintenance of public buildings and grounds. The GMCs collect
refuse from ger areas, apartment and public buildings and private and state-controlled
industrial organizations. In ger areas, GMCs make bulk collections from local disposal
areas and some individual lots. There are also many informal tip sites from which GMCs
also collect refuse.
2.25  As for water and sewerage and other utilities, apartment occupants are billed each
month for solid waste services at a per capita rate of Tug 50 per apartment. Ger residents
are supposed to pay a collection fee determined by MUB/duureg, to ger leaders or
nominated collectors. The fees are passed to GMC, often via individual drivers. The
collection rate from the ger is relatively low and results in constantly declining refuse
collection amounts within ger areas.  There can be no improvement unless the
municipality allocates money from its general tax budget, or causes the duuregs and
khoroos to improve the solid waste fee collection. Assurances were obtained at
negotiations that MUB will undertake a study to determine appropriate alternatives to
fund ger area refuse collection; by July 1, 1998, submit the study and recommendations
to the Association for comments; and by December 31, 1998, prepare an action plan
acceptable to the Association to implement the agreed recommendations.
2.26 NUUTS operates three disposal areas, all virtually unregulated dumping grounds
with no charges levied for dumping. NUUTS appears to be financed entirely by MUB.
A section within the MUB Construction and Planning Department monitors activities of
the duureg GMCs and works with duureg solid waste inspectors. It also can advise on
new collection methods and ensure that new developments are included in collection
schedules. This section also helps to clean up materials around new buildings.



- 16-
3. PROJECT IMPLEMENTING AGENCIES
A. MUNICIPALITY OF ULAANBAATAR
3.1   The proposed project would be implemented by the Municipality of Ulaanbaatar
(MUB). The current Mayor of Ulaanbaatar and his City Council took office in
September 1996, and moved quickly to reorganize the city administration and to
introduce pricing reforms. One aim of the reorganization was to centralize in the
Department of Strategic Planning responsibilities for urban services, formerly dispersed
among four separate departments. This centralization would facilitate implementation of
investments and programs to improve urban services in  accordance with the
Government's sector strategy as well as the Municipality's own four-year development
plan 1997-2000. Chart 1 shows the new municipal structure.
3.2   Of the four departments directly under the Mayor, the Department of Strategic
Planning and the Department under the General Manager are directly relevant to the
operations of municipal services. The General Manager's Department oversees activities
of all utilities under the Municipality, whether autonomous or separate departments with
their own budget. The General Manager monitors (a) USAG, which delivers water
supply and sewerage services; (b) Ulaanbaatar Housing and Communal Services
Company (OSNAAK) and its affiliate kantors, responsible for the housing sector and
maintenance activities; (c) six general maintenance companies responsible for solid
wastes collection in each duureg and NUUTS, the waste disposal company (para. 2.24);
and (d) bus companies. The Department of Strategic Planning controls four offices and
one Bureau: the Construction and Planning Office, in charge of infrastructure and
building designs; the Building and Investment Office, for large development projects; the
Land Office for definition of land use, and land allocation; and the Finance Bureau,
involved in of tariff revision. The municipal organization also includes representatives of
the six municipal districts (duureg), each encompassing both the apartments in the central
city and ger areas on the perimeter. Various services fall within the responsibilities of the
Municipality and the districts (see below). The duuregs are divided into khoroos, the
smallest administrative unit of the city government, headed by elected leaders. Since the
khoroo leaders are usually personally acquainted with most people living in their area,
they make excellent community contact points for local consultation and participation.
3.3   The Municipality formerly obtained most of its revenue from the operation of
companies and utilities under its control.  With the gradual loss of state-owned
enterprises, the corporatization of utilities and the withdrawal of subsidies and cost-free
projects following the fall of the Soviet Union, it now has little revenue to spend on
capital investments, either to renovate deteriorating infrastructure or to construct new
infrastructure to support city growth or to service the expanding ger areas. All land is



- 17-
currently owned by the Government, although arrangements are being made to privatize
ownership of individual apartments and to give long-term leases to registered ger plot-
holders. The prices of housing and the leases and whether they will form the basis of a
property tax have still to be decided by MUB.
3.4   Broad policies on land ownership and borrowing levels for the Municipality and
its Corporations are controlled by the Central Government and the Ministers of Finance
and Economic Development.  Within these constraints, the Municipality has a
considerable degree of autonomy for planning, provision and management of services.
3.5   The movement away from command and control in the Municipality's operations
to one where some services are delivered by largely autonomous agencies (e.g.,
electricity), while these remain government-delivered (roads, health and education), is
posing new challenges. These relate to financing municipal government and planning
and coordination of services, including an effective budgeting and financial management
system to ensure implementation of plans. Moreover, the emergence of democratic local
government and the orderly development of ger areas will require much stronger
participation of community and submunicipal (duureg and khoroo) levels of government
in decisions on matters such as zoning and land-use controls, and levels and pricing of
services. The proposed project's infrastructure development activities in five ger areas
would test this participatory approach for use in other areas.
3.6   To prepare itself for the challenges ahead, the Municipality plans to carry out a
study with funding sought from bilateral sources, of UB's long-term urban management
and planning strategy. That study would propose physical, institutional and financial
strategies to guide UB's future development, and to strengthen the capabilities of the
Municipality, city residents and the private sectors to plan and manage the city on an
ongoing basis. The outputs of the study will be: (a) a growth strategy and service plan
for the metropolitan region; (b) an institutional strengthening plan; and (c) a financial
management plan. Also included in the study is preparation of prototype ger area layouts,
including social services, infrastructure and alternative plot sites.  The alternatives
identified would be costed and the affordability to families of a plot development fund
assessed. Even now, UNDP is supporting a sustainable development program for
Mongolia-Agenda 2001, addressing urban planning, development, and environmental
issues. The study is expected to provide a national strategy that is similar to but broader
than the MUB study.
3.7   Study findings should assist the Municipality to continue its pursuit of market-
oriented policy reform. During the past year, MUB's commitment to economic
principles has been illustrated by authorizing long overdue increases in some utility
charges, including the water tariff, to cover operating costs, and formulating plans to
privatize apartments. It now intends to catalyze reforms in the water supply sector by
establishing USAG as a state-owned company under the 1995 Partnership and Company
Law. This and other reforms are changing the Municipality's role from one of direct
control over municipal services to one of policy setting and monitoring, with its policy



- 18 -
goals reflected in the strategic plans of the corporatized municipal services and in
provision of annual performance agreements concluded with the managers of those
services. Various funding agencies outside the scope of this project have expressed
interest in assisting the Municipality to implement the findings of the urban planning
study and to assist MUB in making the transition from direct provision of services to
oversight.
B. AGENCIES INVOLVED IN THE MUNICIPAL WATER SUPPLY SECTOR
3.8   The Municipality provides water supply and sewerage services through USAG,
which produces, treats and distributes water and collects and treats sewage. USAG
directly services government buildings, schools, hospitals, commercial premises and
some industries. However, domestic consumers receive USAG water through two other
organizations. A government-owned housing company, OSNAAK, and its affiliated
kantors (financially autonomous Housing and Communal Services Units) distribute water
to apartments, while TANK, a water tanker organization, services the ger areas. USAG is
fully owned by MUB as are OSNAAK, the kantors and TANK.
USAG
3.9   Powers and Authority. USAG was established and registered as a separate
company under the 1991 Economic Entities Law. USAG operates and maintains the
wells, pumps, primary distribution and reservoirs in the water supply system as well as
the pipes and treatment plant in the sewerage system (see Chart 2 for its organizational
structure). Secondary networks and service to the apartments is not under its jurisdiction.
System planning and capital investments, coverage of operational losses (if any) and
setting of tariffs and charges are all the responsibility of MUB. Although USAG
maintains separate accounts, as if it were a separate, autonomous, municipally-owned
company, and claims freedom to set its own wages and salaries, USAG operates narrowly
as if it were MUB's water supply and sewerage operations and maintenance department.
USAG's lack of autonomy over its operations has provided it with little incentive to take
responsibility for system operations, strategic planning and financial performance.
Consequently, USAG's analysis of existing and proposed water consumption and tariff
revenue is sketchy, particularly for industries and institutions. There is little technical
analysis of the water network to determine its capacity, shortcomings and efficiency.
And USAG seems to have little involvement in dealing with the related issues of system
water loss, the charging methods, tariff structure and revenue. An institutional study
carried out during project preparation indicated that USAG needed greater status and a
strengthened organization to become more efficient. The Municipality has concurred
with the recommendations of the study. To strengthen its accountability and encourage
performance improvements, USAG would be corporatized so that it may develop into a
somewhat more autonomous public utility with a clear mandate and legal status.
3.10  Staffing and Organizational Structure. USAG has a staff of 1,074 (including
442 in TANK). Using the traditional indicator of a water supply entity's efficiency, i.e.,



- 19 -
staff per system length, USAG appears to be overstaffed, with 1.66 persons per km of
network operated compared to the 0.45 staff per km typical of an efficient organization.
USAG is headed by a Director, under whom are a Chief Engineer responsible for all
system operation, and Heads of Departments for Material Supply, Planning and Finance,
Personnel and Consumers. Also part of USAG, but in fact operating almost separately
from it, is TANK. USAG's flat organizational structure indicates that decision-making is
centralized in its Director. With corporatization, the Director will be required to assume
more responsibilities for a more independent organization, and he will need to rely on a
strong management team  with which decision-making can be shared.  USAG's
restructuring into a more pyramidal organization is therefore recommended. Any
restructuring should also include the separation of USAG's now combined water and
sewerage operations, so that USAG can identify the costs and efficiency of each service
and the adequacy of tariffs for that service.
3.11  Maintenance.  Due to the insufficient tariff increases authorized by the
Municipality in the past, USAG has lacked adequate revenue to carry out system
maintenance. It currently has no regular maintenance procedures, and maintenance is
usually performed only when absolutely necessary. Now, with the revenues expected
from a substantial tariff increase in October 1996, funds should become available for a
much stepped-up maintenance program. Technical Assistance for developing adequate
maintenance purchase programs and procedures is included under the Project. During
negotiations, assurance was obtained that USAG will establish and operate a separate
maintenance account to provide an adequate level of maintenance and repair for its
facilities, and increase expenditures on maintenance in real terms starting in the fiscal
year beginning January 1, 1998, at IO percent in real terms, during the project period
3.12  Billing. About 1,060 institutional consumers, including industries, are billed
directly by USAG, which either reads meters, where they are installed and functioning,
or, for industrial consumers, bills them according to detailed consumption norms
determined by the type of organization and activity. While the current norms for
industrial users may closely reflect actual consumption, the system suffers from an
inherent lack of incentives to conserve water and should be replaced by metered billing to
charge actual volume consumed.
3.13  The introduction of metered billing is even more of a priority for apartment units.
The average norm of 150 lcd for billing to apartment dwellers is far below the actual
amount supplied, which surveys indicate is around 410 lcd. The bulk of the water over
the norm is simply wasted through leaking toilets and taps. The economic and financial
loss from this wastage is unsustainably high and should be stopped by an incentive
system to encourage conservation combined with repair of poorly functioning facilities
(see Annex 4). In addition to the installation of water meters at each apartment core,
replacement or installation of meters on USAG's own customers is included under the
project.



- 20 -
3.14  Financial Performance.   As discussed further in Chapter 5, despite
hyperinflation of about 5,700 percent overall in 1991-95, USAG was able to contain its
losses to 3 percent of total revenue in 1994 and to realize a small profit in 1995 by a
combination of tariff adjustments and reduction of operations in real terms. This
financial achievement masked serious problems in that USAG avoided major losses in
part by capping wages and almost totally neglecting system maintenance and investment.
The latter has sharply reduced the economic life of most assets, and rehabilitation of
facilities is now badly needed. In addition to the installation of water meters at each
apartment core, replacement or installation of meters on USAG's own customers is
included under the Project.
3.15  Effective in October 1996, USAG raised its water tariff to apartment dwellers by a
weighted average of 83 percent. This base tariff level, adjusted in the future for inflation,
would be adequate for USAG's operations in the medium term, until it needs to service
debt on the proposed credit or other future loans. Even with the increase, USAG's unit
charge to relatively poor ger area residents in 1997 is regressive by being about 12 times
higher than the unit charge to better-off apartment dwellers. The high charge also
prevents ger dwellers from consuming the minimum level of water required for health
and hygiene. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that the gap between tariffs
to apartment and ger area residents will be phased out so that all consumers will pay the
same tariff when the piped water supply becomes available to ger area residents.
TANK
3.16 The TANK "Company" is a separate suborganization within USAG. It is
responsible for distributing water to ger areas, for which purpose it owns a fleet of 49
tanker trucks (mostly 5,000-liter capacity, only about 30 operational), tanker filling
stations and 262 sales kiosks. TANK has 442 staff. Kiosk attendants employed by
TANK dispense water in exchange for tokens TANK sells to consumers, charging Tug 8
per 20 liters. TANK, like USAG, is treated for accounting purposes as a separate entity,
but it functions only as a water supply and maintenance operation, with a quasi-
independent identity in relation to USAG. With respect to tariffs, investment and
operational policy, and sources of investment funds, it is wholly under MUB's direction.
TANK is not a separate income and expenditure center within USAG as it does not
purchase, or meter the water that it obtains from USAG for distribution to ger consumers.
3.17 USAG has insufficient control over TANK, which functions as a partly separate
organization within USAG. Due to its unsustainably high operating costs, TANK has
been losing money despite the very high tariff to ger residents and free water supply from
USAG. USAG has covered these losses, with the result that TANK is its second largest
item of expenditure after electricity, which accounts for over 70 percent of operating
costs. TANK's unit operating cost for water in 1996 constant terms is Tug 535/mr,
compared with Tug 33/m3 for the piped water system. The current arrangement also
appears open to waste and abuse as an analysis suggests that water supply through tank
operation seems underreported by half. Business hours at water kiosks are irregular and



- 21 -
need to be regulated. The analysis of TANK's operations (Annex 12) shows that
replacing the tankered water supply with a piped supply, as would be done under the
proposed project, can significantly reduce USAG's operating costs while more than
doubling ger area resident's water supply to 20 lcd. Assurances were obtained during
negotiations that the TANK activities will become integrated with those of USA G by the
time of completion of USAG's establishment as a corporation under Mongolia's 1995
Partnership and Company Law and no later than December 31, 1997. With this, TANK
staff would come under the control of appropriate USAG departments.
OSNAAKIKantors
3.18 Some 750 municipally-owned apartment buildings are managed by OSNAAK, a
wholly municipally-owned housing holding "Company." OSNAAK functions through
18 kantors, which actually operate and maintain the buildings and collects rents, provide
service connections for water and electricity, and collect utility user-charges that they
remit to the appropriate agency after retaining a service charge. OSNAAK and the
kantors are barely able to generate sufficient income to maintain the current apartments,
let alone to construct new units. Each kantor is responsible for an area of the city and
functions essentially as a housing management company for that area. Though in theory
OSNAAK and the kantors are financially independent, self-sustaining business
enterprises, they lack autonomy, and all major decisions affecting their operation, like the
determination of rents, capital investment, etc., are made by MUB. OSNAAK's total
manpower of around 60 consists essentially of advisers and supervisors monitoring the
kantors; the kantors have their staffing numbers established according to the number of
apartments they serve. The kantors are capable of carrying out plumbing repairs and
water meter installation under the proposed project.
3.19  The kantor housing estates receive USAG piped cold water and-via CTPs-hot
water for apartments and institutional premises. Prior to January 1997, MUB allowed the
kantors to collect USAG and other utility charges from apartments and institutions. For
collecting the water charges, the kantors retained about 25 percent of gross revenues. The
high percentage retained was apparently an indirect means of enhancing kantor revenues
to cover rising costs of operating and maintaining the (deteriorating) housing units and
their utilities in the absence of adequate increases in highly subsidized rents. However, as
decided by MUB, beginning January 1997, USAG receives all revenue collected by the
kantors as water and sewer charges, and the kantors can charge tenants an additional 20
percent of the USAG water and sewerage charge as a similar fee. The present system of
the kantors collecting apartments on behalf of USAG remains a satisfactory interim
solution, until USAG develops its own capacity and installs an adequate billing system.
C. SECTOR IMPROVEMENT ACTION PLAN
Municipal Objectives For the Sector
3.20 Both the Central Government and MUB agree on the need to renew and improve
urban services in the capital. For solid wastes, MUB believes that cost recovery is the



- 22 -
most appropriate form if ensuring revenues to cover operations, but wishes to review
principals and method of settling a collection fee. For water and sanitation, MUB seeks
to correct the most pressing problems and to introduce commercial principles in USAG,
thus extending to this sector the reforms being introduced in the city bus company and,
more boldly, in other areas of the economy. The Municipality has three major sectoral
goals. Regarding pricing and cost recovery, it intends to replace the current normative
system of charging with a quantitative system based on metered volumes and to fully
recover by user charges USAG's operating costs and the larger of its depreciation or debt
service costs. With respect to institutional strengthening, MUB supports the conversion
of USAG into a publicly-owned limited liability company with the attributes of a
commercial, self-supporting, revenue-earning firm, displaying operating and financial
efficiency. And on equity considerations, MUB seeks to equalize unit charges to ger and
apartment residents, at least for basic levels of water service.
Sector and USAG Action Plans
3.21  These objectives have been incorporated into Action Plans, one for USAG and
one for other sector issues and agencies. The Plans, adopted by the Municipality for
implementation over the next five years, and by USAG for its own jurisdiction. were
discussed and agreed at negotiations and incorporated in the Minutes of Negotiations.
3.22  The main elements of the Sector Improvement Action Plan are outlined in Annex
5a. Assurances were obtained that MUB would implement or cause USAG to implement
the Action Plans that could be amended by mutual agreement of the Government and IDA
during supervision missions.
3.23  The Institutional Development Plan for USAG's internal reorganization is shown
in Annex 5b. USAG was unable to corporatize under Mongolia's Partnership and
Company Law since it had not complied with two requirements of the Law:
establishment of a governing and an auditing board and submission of Articles of
Association consistent with principles of the Law. USAG has now taken action in these
areas, and is expected to have full legal status as a company by July 1, 1997. USAG
would thereafter gain autonomy in most aspects of its operations, with the exception of
tariff setting, which would remain under municipal control. As a condition of credit
effectiveness, USAG will be established under Mongolia's 1995 Partnership and
Company Law, on the basis of a charter acceptable to the Association.  During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that MUB would cause USAG to implement an
Institutional Development Plan and submit revisions to the Plan to the Association for its
prior approval, as the need arises during project implementation.
3.24  During the project period, USAG would be restructured to enhance efficiency and
cater for its enlarged duties as a result of project activities. Restructuring would be
carried out gradually, in three phases. The first phase would separate USAG's water
supply and wastewater activities, integrate TANK staff into appropriate USAG
departments and have USAG assume TANK's responsibility for collecting water charges



- 23 -
for ger area services. During the second phase, USAG would establish new departments
to handle the new services it would provide, e.g., metering, and would strengthen
departnents with expanded responsibilities, such as the Customer Services Department,
to be responsible for meter reading, billing and collection, and the Water Supply
Maintenance Department, to deal with the ger area water pipelines and to undertake an
expanded maintenance program for the existing network. The final phase of USAG's
action plan, at project completion and thereafter, would cater for further emerging needs,
e.g., assuming responsibility for investment planning and execution should these
functions be delegated to USAG by the municipality. Technical assistance and training
to facilitate USAG's development would be provided under the proposed project.



- 24 -
4. THE PROJECT
A. PROJECT ORIGIN AND OBJECTIVES
4.1   Project Formulation.  In mid-1994, the Government's concern about the
condition of Mongolia's cities, which had begun to show the effects of several years of
forced neglect, caused it to request the Association's assistance in carrying out an
informal review of urban services in Mongolia's main cities. In particular, urban issues
were assessed from the perspective of two government goals: poverty alleviation and
devolution of functions and responsibilities to local governments.
4.2   The subsequent discussion of suggestions and proposals arising from the review
clarified priorities and prompted the Government to seek further Association help in
resolving two urgent issues facing Ulaanbaatar-the deterioration of its water supply and
distribution system and the dearth of public services in ger areas. A Phase I feasibility
study was subsequently prepared with financing provided by Japan's Policy and Human
Resources Development Fund (PHRD), followed by Phase II preparation of detailed
designs under Australian AusAID funding. Throughout each phase a Steering Committee
composed of representatives of various ministries assisted and guided the consultants and
organized the participation of local area leaders in the selection and design of
components. At a full review of the proposal in October 1996, the Municipality of
Ulaanbaatar endorsed the project scope and confirmed its contribution to the project's
financing plan.
4.3   Overall Project Objectives. The project's general objectives are to assist the
Government to: (a) address the inequity of services between ger and formal housing
areas, thereby improving the quality of life of the low-income population; (b) restore
levels and quality of water supply services in the Municipality and make that supply
accessible to the ger areas; and (c) develop more efficient and autonomous, performance-
oriented institutions, with a view to eventual privatization. Project activities in UB are
intended to demonstrate to Mongolia's other large cities the modalities and benefits of
urban renewal in the expectation that they will replicate these initiatives themselves the
future.
4.4   Detailed Project Objectives. The project's specific objectives are to:
(a)   Improve the quality, quantity and reliability of water supply for about 70
percent of the population in predominantly low-income ger areas, where
about half of UB's population resides;



- 25 -
(b)   Substantially lower the unit cost of water supply to ger areas by replacing
tankered with piped water and thereby bring the unit price for water
charged to the ger in line with that paid by apartment dwellers;
(c)   Improve ger area infrastructure, including roads, bridges, footpaths and
storm drainage, and enhance ger access to water supply and sanitation;
(d)   Arrest the deterioration in, and improve the reliability and quality of, the
city water distribution system while adapting it for distribution of piped
water to ger areas;
(e)   Reduce water wastage, the production of nonrevenue-producing water, and
water production costs;
(f)   Replace the current norm-based water and sanitation billing system to one
that is quantity-based; and
(g)   Develop USAG's capabilities for financial and operational management
and convert it into a self-financing commercially-oriented public utility.
B. PROJECT IMPACT INDICATORS
4.5   Project implementation progress and development impact would be monitored by
a set of key indicators showing the delivery of inputs and realization of expected outputs.
The following indicators, shown in detail in Annex 3, would complement operational and
financial performance targets in the project's implementation, procurement and
disbursement schedules and project conditionality.
(a)   Poverty Alleviation
* Number and percentage of water kiosks served by a piped supply
versus targets
*  Ratio of the unit price of water to ger areas and to apartments to
become one by end of project period
*  Number of ger plots with access to footpathlroad within 50 meters
*  Per capita ger consumption of water
*  Growth of Ger Infrastructure Account
(b)   Efficiency Enhancement
* Decline in per capita water consumption in apartment buildings
* Amount of water delivered by truck



- 26 -
* Ratio of population served by USAG water supply to total number of
USAG staff
(c)   Capacity Building
* Water meters installed under the project
* Actual vs. planned budget expenditures for system maintenance
* Ratio of USAG's operating revenue to expenditures
(d)   Implementation Progress
* Actual disbursement compared to targets
* Planned vs. actual bid documents prepared by each December and
awarded by next April, to meet construction season requirements.
C. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
4.6   The proposed project would include a physical investment program and an
institutional development component as outlined below and described in detail in Anne
2.
(a)   Physical Investments:
(i)   Strengthening  the  water supply  distribution  network  by
constructing about 32 km of transmission mains (300/500 mm
diameter) for the existing system and extending the system by
about 12 km with connection mains serving four ger areas;
(ii)    Constructing four concrete reservoirs to store water intended for
ger areas at Hailast, Baiyankoshuu, Yarmag and Shar Khad;
(iii)   Supplying and laying about 98 km of specially insulated PVC
pipelines to service most public water kiosks in about 12 ger areas;
(iv)   Implementing a water leakage and wastage reduction program
through: (a) the supply and installation of about 22,000 building
water meters to monitor and record building core consumption of
hot and cold water; and (b) repair of building and apartment
plumbing fixtures;
(v)    Supplying equipment and tools for maintenance and meter repair
workshops;



- 27 -
(vi)   Providing sewer observation and cleaning equipment to assess,
clear blockages to, and clean sewers, that have been damaged
during flooding and freezing periods; and
(vii)    Undertaking a Ger Area Infrastructure Upgrading Program in five
ger areas, entailing construction of limited roadwork, cutoff drains,
footpaths, additional water kiosks, repair of existing kiosks and
community bathrooms and laundries, and provision of garbage
bins.
(b)   Institutional Component:
(i)    Technical assistance in project management during the first two
years of project implementation; and in community awareness and
education of ger area residents;
(ii)   Institutional strengthening of USAG, the UB water utility, through
technical assistance and training in financial management,
technical areas, and development of maintenance programs for
water and wastewater systems;
(iii)    Studies reviewing the long-term development and planning of ger
areas in Ulaanbaatar, and definition of appropriate levels of public
services; and
(iv)    Development of, and training in, a computerized USAG billing and
collection system for metered supplies.
Water Supply Investments
4.7   The proposed water supply investments would address three aspects of USAG's
operations by: (a) improving and removing bottlenecks in the primary distribution
network; (b) constructing additional distribution facilities to improve service in ger areas
and to reduce the access cost to users; and (c) implementation of a water wastage
reduction plan in apartment areas, aimed at bringing overall consumption figures in line
with more typical levels through control by metering and replacement of leaking
plumbing fixtures.
4.8   Distribution Network. To improve service to the city's higher-demand areas,
now receiving inadequate and or intermittent flows and pressure, the proposed project
would make the now underutilized reservoir system fully operational to raise pressure
head, and the primary transmission main system around Ulaanbaatar would be reinforced
to accommodate larger flows at higher pressures. These improvements would raise
service quality without additional, and expensive, pumping. The project would complete
the southern section of the main distribution loop around the city area to help maintain
the water pressure and push flows toward the underserved western part of the city (Map



- 28 -
IBRD 28690). This would mainly entail: (a) supplying and laying pipelines for the
southern transmission main (3 km of 300 mm dia.); (b) constructing links between four
existing reservoirs (18 km of 500/400/300 mm dia.) and the southern links for the city
network main (11 km of 400 mm dia.); (c) rehabilitating and commissioning the
Northeast and "X" reservoirs, coupled with installation of a telemetry system to optimize
reservoir use; (d) repair and replacement of existing valves and installation of regulating
valves to increase pressures on secondary networks presently ill-served; and (e) provision
of equipment and spares for a workshop and laboratory.
4.9   Provision of Piped Water Supply to Ger Areas. Construction of additional
distribution facilities to improve the ger areas and reduce the cost of water to local
inhabitants would require development of reservoirs and networks to serve 12 priority ger
areas in the northeast, northwest and southwest fringes of the city's formally built-up
areas. About 70 percent of the population in the 12 areas (169,000 people) would be
supplied with piped water. These areas are projected to have a total population of
210,000 people by 2010. Six of the 12 areas were considered unsuitable for project
investment due to permafrost encroachment. The project would provide: (a) installation
of about 93 km of specially insulated pipes as the primary water supply distribution
network in ger areas, with risers to the public water kiosks serving the population;
(b) supply and laying of two transmission pipelines (9.4 km of 300/500 mm dia. and
2.7 km of 500 mm dia.) linking the ger distribution system to the existing city network;
(c) construction of four semiburied reservoirs at Hailast, Yarmag, Baiyankoshuu and Shar
Had, totaling 2,500 m3 capacity; (d) drilling of three boreholes at Yarmag Shar Khad and
Baiyankoshuu for the supply of areas that are better economically served through
independent groundwater supply; and (e) construction of 25 new water kiosks and
rehabilitation of 1 10 old ones serving the 12 ger areas.
4.10 Water Wastage Reduction Program. In support of the program, the project
would provide: (a) supply and installation of about 22,000 cold and hot water meters for
apartment building cores; (b) supply of fixtures and piping for apartments and building
interiors; and (c) provision of meter testing and repair facilities. Since water delivery to
