apartment buildings has a complicated configuration owing to the involvement of CTPs,
metering of individual apartments would be costly and at this stage is considered
unnecessary. The project would therefore install meters at the entry of each apartment
block to measure amounts of water used by a group of tenants. The block metering
would thus provide an incentive for all tenants to both reduce water usage and proceed
with repair of broken plumbing fixtures. The water meters would be supplied and owned
by USAG, but installed by the kantors. Equipment needed to repair plumbing fixtures
would be provided to tenants as an additional cost or a loan, at a discounted rate. Kantor
staff would carry out the repairs. Details of the water metering program is described in
Annex 4.



- 29 -
Emergency Sewerage System Repairs
4.11   The project includes a minimal amount of financing for purchase of equipment
needed to investigate and assess the condition of sewers, to clear blockages and to clean
sewers, such as high-pressure jetting and descaling machines. Repairs of servers and
manholes damaged by flood events and street traffic would be undertaken under the
maintenance program.
Ger Area Infrastructure Upgrading Program
4.12  Investments. Infrastructure improvements would be piloted in five priority ger
areas, with the expectation that similar works would subsequently be carried out in the
other ger areas (see Map IBRD 28690). In response to priorities expressed by ger
residents and with a view to affordability, the project would provide: (a) construction of
28.1 km of 1.2 m wide footpaths, 10.8 km of paved or gravel roads and three footbridges
and one road bridge giving residents better access from their plots to public facilities
(water kiosks, bus stops, schools); (b) construction of about 2.6 km of concrete-sided and
bottom drains and 2.2 km of levees, to ensure protection of ger from storms;
(c) manufacture and installation of about 650 three-cubic meter capacity refuse bins for
collection of solid wastes from ger and supply of several tip trucks and compactor trucks
for subsequent evacuation of refuse; and (d) construction of five laundries and bathhouses
in each of the five targeted areas. The detailed design and implementation in each ger
area would be developed with the active participation of the residents, to establish their
sense of ownership of the improvements and their commitment to future maintenance and
cost recovery. Details of the program are discussed in Annex 15.
4.13  The project infrastructure improvements will be made in five ger areas-Gandan,
Hailast, Dambadarjaa, Chingeltei and Denjin-which have been identified as having the
highest priority among the 12 ger areas found suitable for improvement. To establish
priorities for project improvements, the ger areas were ranked according to the following
criteria:
. health conditions and access to health services (e.g., socioeconomic
conditions)
*  population and population density
*  availability of services
*  logistical conditions for services' (e.g., ground conditions, permafrost, slope,
erosion and access, building relocation or property acquisition requirements,
which were given 50 percent weight in the analysis)
*  town planning and future city development requirements



- 30 -
community willingness to participate in identification of priority investments
and to pay ger improvement charges for them.
4.14  About 75,000 people or 16,800 households reside in the five ger areas targeted for
infrastructure upgrading under the project. These areas would also benefit from the
project's provision of a piped water supply to the 12 priority ger areas. Due to resource
constraints, only about 45 percent of the population in the 12 ger areas suitable for
improvement could be included in the infrastructure upgrading program; and only a
minimum of essential improvements can be made in these areas. A substantial amount of
improvement work remains to be done in the future when more resources can be made
available. Agreements were obtained at negotiations that MUB would carry out a Ger
Infrastructure Improvement Program acceptable to the Association and would submit
any revision to such a program to the Association for its prior approval.
4.15  Planning Study. As an initial step in the process of improving the city's land use
management and integrating ger areas into the overall urban development plan, a study
would be carried out for the medium-term development and planning of ger areas in UB,
including definition of appropriate levels of public services. The study would also
consider the implications for ger planning of the city's recent land tenure proposals,
which would seem to grant ger residents user rights over their plots for a specific, fixed
period, which remains to be determined. Assurances were obtained at negotiations that
MUB would carry out a study regarding medium-term development and planning of ger
areas, including appropriate levels of public investment, in accordance with terms of
reference acceptable to IDA, by December 31, 1998 and take the recommendations
arisingfrom the study into account in its urban development planning.
4.16  Infrastructure Changes. About 50 percent of the proposed ger investments-
including small roads and culverts, footpaths, grading, and solid waste bins-would be
subject to an on-site infrastructure charge, to permit cost recovery and sustainability of
the improvement program. The charge, to be about $75 equivalent per plot, would be
partly paid over time by the beneficiaries. An affordable charge of Tug 500/plot/month
($0.68/month), equivalent to about 1 percent of average 1996 ger household income,
would pay off major ger on-site costs in eight years (Annex 15). The charges would be
deposited in a separate account for ger improvement, which would be maintained by
MUB and collected by the khoroos.  The fee would have several benefits by:
(a) allowing the repayments to be used for further improvements to or maintenance in ger
area and to strengthen local community organizations; (b) financing MUB's
improvements in new ger areas accounting; (c) encouraging the residents' commitment to
the project improvements through an affordable charge; and (d) attracting additional
funding from external agencies. Subsequent area improvements would be prioritized and
undertaken according to the criteria mentioned in para. 4.13.



- 31 -
Institutional Development
4.17  The project would finance technical assistance in project management during the
first two years of implementation; support to USAG; and community awareness and
education of ger area residents. During negotiations, assurances were obtained that
MUB would recruit consultants for the project with terms and conditions satisfactory to
the Association.  Recruitment of consultants for project management would be a
condition of credit effectiveness.
4.18  While the Mongolian construction industry has a reasonable experience in
constructing physical works, the Municipal Construction Planning Department has very
limited experience in managing Bank Group projects. Technical assistance would
therefore be provided under the project to assist local staff to gain skills and experience
with project management issues, particularly procurement, disbursement and monitoring.
Specialists to be recruited would include a project management advisor, a cold-climate
construction advisor, a water supply construction advisor, and a water supply
communications/control system advisor. The consultant team would work with an MUB
counterpart team over the course of their contract. Terms of reference for these and other
consultants to be engaged for the project are given in Annex 6.
4.19  During the project period, USAG would reorganize itself and carry out a program
of institutional strengthening to allow it to function as a commercially-oriented water
supply and sewerage utility, with responsibilities for investment planning and execution,
and financial control. A USAG Institutional Development Action Plan, developed by
USAG and its consultants, is shown in Annex 5. The proposed project would finance
technical assistance and training to help USAG implement the Plan, with expertise to be
provided in financial management, technical areas, and development of maintenance
programs for water and wastewater systems.  Development of and training in a
computerized USAG billing and collection system for metered supplies would also be
financed.
4.20  The Ger Area Infrastructure Improvement Program is intended to demonstrate the
benefits of the proposed investments to other ger areas. Its success will depend on
community commitment to and participation in the works to be carried out. To sustain
the local participation shown during project preparation, it is proposed to engage a local
nongovernmental organization, Women for Social Progress, to stimulate community
awareness of the project's aims, mobilize and organize community involvement in
carrying out construction activities, and organize community awareness of public health
and project issues.
D. STATUS OF PROJECT PREPARATION
4.21  The Project Preparation Office and key project staff are receiving assistance from
consultants involved in the Phase II preparation; study and designs have been completed
for all physical works to be carried out during the first year of project implementation.
To avoid losing one construction season (May-November 1997), bid documentation is



- 32 -
now finalized to ensure readiness of bidding documents by June 1997. Also, an extensive
canvassing to determine precise requirements for the water meter installation and fixture
repair programs is under way with the kantors' input, and should yield information for
defining equipment and work requirements. A first canvassing of interested contractors
and suppliers has taken place through national public notification as well as circulation of
the notice for the first year's works to commercial sections of all represented embassies in
Ulaanbaatar of IDA member countries. The Municipality is eager to proceed with early
implementation of physical works and has earmarked Tug 100 million ($140,000) to start
contracts with advance payment from the Municipality and subsequent reimbursement
from the Credit and the Grant.
E. COSTS AND FINANCING
Project Cost
4.22  The estimated cost of the proposed project is $23.6 million, including import
duties and sales taxes, and physical and price contingencies. All cost and financing
estimates, including local costs, have been calculated in US dollars, due to high and
unpredictable domestic inflation. Cost estimates are based on engineering designs and on
unit prices prevailing in 1997. Physical contingencies are calculated at 10 percent for all
goods and civil works, and 5 percent for technical assistance. Price escalation is based on
a projected international inflation rate of 2.5 percent per year for 1997 and thereafter.
Cost estimates are summarized in Table 4.1 below, and detailed costs are provided in
Annex.

Project Financing
4.23  The financing plan is shown in Table 4.2. Total financing required is $23.6
million of which, $14.8 million, or 67 percent, constitutes the direct foreign exchange
component. The proposed IDA Credit of SDR 12.3 million ($16.7 million equivalent)
represents 100 percent of the project's foreign exchange component and 71 percent of the
total project cost. The IDA credit would finance the project's water supply and sewerage
components and related technical assistance. An Australian Government AusAID grant
of $4.9 million equivalent would finance 100 percent of the ger area infrastructure
upgrading works and related technical assistance. MUB would finance $2.0 million
equivalent, or 8 percent of total costs. The AusAID grant would be managed by IDA
under the Global Trust Fund Agreement between the Bank Group and the Government of
Australia.

4.24 IDA funds would be made available to the Government of Mongolia at standard
IDA terms, with 40 years' maturity and a 10-year grace period. The credit proceeds
would be onlent by the Government to MUB under a subsidiary loan agreement between
the Government and MUB. A further onlending agreement would be made between
MUB and USAG. AusAID funds would be made available to the Government, which
would pass on the funds to MUB under a Subsidiary Grant Agreement. Assurances were
obtained at negotiations from the Borrower that: (a) it would onlend the IDA credit to
MUB under a Subsidiary Loan Agreement at an annual interest rate of I percent and a
repayment period of 40 years including 10 years of grace, (b) MUB would onlend
proceeds of the IDA Credit to USAG under a Subsidiary Loan Agreement at an annual
interest rate of 5 percent and a repayment period of 20 years including 5 years of grace,
with MUB bearing the exchange risk. Execution of the Subsidiary Loan Agreements
would be a condition of credit effectiveness.
4.25 A Project Agreement would be executed between IDA and MUB.

F. PROCUREMENT
4.26  Project procurement would be in accordance with the "Bank's Guidelines for
Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits, January 1995" and revised in January
and August 1996.   All international competitive bidding (ICB) documents would
conform to the Bank's Standard Bidding Documents. Under previous IDA operations in
Mongolia, procurement has been centralized within a unit in the Ministry of Trade and
Industry now transferred to MID, where experience and expertise have been developed to
assist project entities in carrying out procurement in conformity with IDA guidelines.
Procurement under the proposed project would benefit from MID's assistance, as well as
consultant assistance to be provided under the project to MUB and USAG. In the
absence of specific local bidding contracts, the consultants who assisted the Borrower in
preparing the project have helped to prepare satisfactory model civil works documents for
small works in the Mongolian language on the basis of the Bank Sample Bid Documents.
Annex 8 lists the Schedule of Procurement Packages and the method of procurement, and
Table 4.3 summarizes the procurement categories and the expected procurement methods.

4.27  Civil works contracts totaling about $11.4 million would be packaged so that
each contract could be implemented over a period of seven months, April to October,
when construction is possible in Mongolia. Contracts above $0.5 million each would be
procured under ICB. About five ICB contracts, each averaging $0.7 million and totaling
some $3.3 million, are envisioned. Qualified domestic contractors would receive a 7.5
percent preference in bid evaluation on these contracts. Contracts above $0.2 million
each, and expected to total $6.6 million for both the water supply and ger upgrading
components, would be awarded through National Competitive Bidding (NCB) procedures
acceptable to the Association, with bidding also open to foreign contractors from
neighboring countries who are likely to tender for these smaller contracts. Nearby
foreign contractors would be allowed to participate in NCB since Mongolia currently has
only about 12 competent local civil works contractors, some of whom occasionally
associate with foreign contractors. Civil works contracts for installation of meters and
plumbing fixture repair amounting to $20,000 each and aggregating to $200,000 would
be carried out under force account or direct contracting with kantors' work forces.
Equipment and materials for the water supply and ger upgrading components would total
about $4.8 million. All contracts above $100,000 would be awarded after ICB. About
four ICB equipment packages, each costing an estimated $1 million, are expected.
Contracts averaging $100,000 each would be awarded following NCB, and are expected
to total $500,000. Contracts of less than $60,000 each for yearly supplies of various
minor equipment, which cannot be economically tendered under ICB/NCB packages,
would be bid through International Shopping in an aggregate amount not exceeding
$300,000. There are no manufacturers of relevant sector equipment in Mongolia, and
domestic preference would not be offered. There would be no prequalification of bidders
for goods or works contracts, but the annual updated procurement notice published in
local newspapers for informing contractors and commercial sections of embassies of each
year's bidding packages will request interested contractors to state their qualifications and
experience for each type of contract. Postqualification would be carried out for each
contract during bid evaluation.
4.28 IDA-financed training and technical assistance services ($1.7 million) would be
procured in accordance with IDA Guidelines for the Selection and Employment of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers, January 1997. The most appropriate selection
method would be through quality- and cost-based selection (QCBS), which combines the
quality of the proposal with the cost of the services. However, specific assignments
(audit, maintenance system development, PMU strengthening) totaling $450,000 would
be procured through quality-based selection (QBS), least cost selection, single source
selection, and individual consultants.
4.29  All contracts above $100,000 for equipment and civil works would be subject to
prior review. This would cover more than 80 percent of the total value of Association-
financed contracts. All consulting assignments above $50,000 for firms and above
$20,000 for individuals would also be subject to prior review. In addition, post review
would be carried out of one contract in five until the mid-term review in December 1999
and thereafter one contract in ten if earlier reviews have justified such reductions. All



- 36 -
terms of reference for single-source selections would also be subject to IDA's prior
review.
4.30  Early Procurement. The Municipality is about to construct two water supply
transmission mains, expected to cost about $1.2 million equivalent, to improve water
distribution to the city and, more important, to ger areas. While the method of procuring
these works does not fully accord with Bank Group requirements, since procurement was
limited to municipally selected companies, the works are nevertheless considered as part
of the project and their funding by the municipality as part of the Government's
contribution to project financing.

the credit closing date would be June 30, 2002.

H. PROJECT MANAGEMENT AND IMPLEMENTATION
4.33  The Municipality of Ulaanbaatar would be responsible for implementing the
project. While the Municipality has implemented some relatively large civil works
during the past few years, the proposed project, with its annual work program equivalent
to the city's total annual capital expenditure, would require provision of additional
management support.  This would be financed under the project.  Additional
strengthening in municipal financial management would be provided by bilateral
assistance now being identified.
4.34 To oversee day-to-day aspects of implementation, a Project Management Unit
(PMU) would be established in the Department of Strategic Planning under the Mayor's
Office. The PMU would comprise five professional staff and appropriate support
personnel. Consultants in project management to be provided under the project would
assist the Unit during the first 24 months of implementation, and technical expertise in
the water supply sector would be provided during the project's first two years. As a
condition of effectiveness, MUB will establish the PMU and thereafter maintain it
throughout project implementation, with budget, functions and responsibilities
satisfactory to the Association, and with competent staff in adequate numbers.
4.35  Implementation of the Ger Area Infrastructure Improvement Program would be
carried out by MUB through its local officials in the neighborhood khoroos. The
planning and design of improvements beyond Year 1 proposals would be the
responsibility of MUB's Architecture and Building Office, which would coordinate with
khoroos for the purpose of community involvement (para. 4.42). The meter installation
and fixture repair programs would be carried out by the kantors' own labor force. Minor
technical assistance would be provided under the meter supply contract to ensure
appropriate installation of meters, and preparation of a satisfactory computerized billing
system, enabling USAG and kantors to start effective monitoring of customer
consumption upon installation of the meters.
4.36  A Project Implementation Plan (PIP) prepared by MUB has been prepared and is
available in the project file (Annexi1). An Implementation Schedule is providing the
detailed procurement plan for the first two project years is included in Annex 1O. As part
of the sector improvement action plan, MUB and USAG agreed during negotiations to
have PMU prepare and submit by each October 15 a procurement plan for the next year.
I. LAND ACQUISITION AND RESETTLEMENT
4.37  No land acquisition is required for the project. MUB already owns land for the
project reservoirs, and the two transmission mains would be laid in existing streets. The
pipelines serving the ger areas and the infrastructure improvements to those areas would
similarly entail no disruption to the local inhabitants since the ger areas are very well laid
out, with major access ways that will easily accommodate the water pipes, water kiosks,
and other small infrastructure to be provided under the project. No resettlement or
disruption to current economic activities is therefore expected.



- 38 -
J. ENVIRONMENTAL CONSIDERATIONS
4.38  Environmental Assessment. For purposes of Environmental Assessment (EA),
the proposed project is designated as Category B.  With support from  technical
assistance, MID and the Ministry of Nature and Environment prepared an EA for the
project, which focused on the construction of a small concrete reservoir on a hilltop to
serve the local ger population. The EA concludes that only minor controls are required,
particularly during the construction stage, to ensure that the proposed reservoir is
environmentally sound. The site geology has been investigated by borehole drilling
across the site, the existing environment is already disturbed in terms of vehicles and foot
traffic, and there will be insignificant impact on fauna and flora. The assessment includes
an Environmental Management Plan covering all phases of the development from
preconstruction to operation and maintenance includes mitigation measures for the few
potential impacts identified-noise and dust during construction as well as soil erosion
and silt runoff.   During negotiations, assurances were be obtained that the
Environmental Management Plan would be carried out in a manner satisfactory to IDA.
4.39  More positively, project activities to reduce water wastage, e.g., construction of a
reservoir and provision of water meters, will maximize the availability of water and allow
service to the ger areas and other additional users within this relatively water-short area.
They will also obviate the need to increase water production in the next five years while
at the same time making more water available to the ger population. The increase of
water consumption of ger residents from 7.5-10 lcd now to 20 lcd by 2010 is unlikely to
cause any waterlogging situation within or around ger plots.  The project would
significantly improve the environment in ger areas by reducing solid waste via its
improved collection and disposal and by reducing flooding and erosion through improved
stormwater drainage. The reduction of wastewater due to the water wastage reduction
program will also help improve the water quality downstream, and thus reduce demands
on the already very limited operational capacity of UB's wastewater treatment plant.
4.40  Cold Climate Considerations.  The project design takes into consideration
Ulaanbaatar's severe winter climate, which is comparable to that of Anchorage, Alaska.
The main potential problem is freezing of water in pipework systems, particularly in ger
areas where low flows can be expected with the low water demand. Frost penetration
could exceed 5.5 meters in the city and 6 to 8 meters in northern exposed ger areas.
However, a cost comparison indicates that it is more economical to insulate pipes and
prevent stagnation than to lay small pipes below the frost penetration line. Specific
design features were therefore adopted for the pipe insulation, pipe material, burial depth
and coverage of pipelines and rises serving kiosks, flow patterns in distribution pipes and
reservoirs, looped connections to water-delivering kiosks, minimization of dead-end
pipes, introduction of special drainage, and control and maintenance features in case of
system failures and need of pipe repairs. Specific investments such as a recirculating ger
looped network and boiler r-calculation near and around reservoirs have been included in
the project. These measures are based on similar systems in operations in Northern
Canada and Alaska and have been found acceptable by a cold climate construction expert.



- 39 -
K. COMMUNITY PARTICIPATION
4.41   Throughout project preparation process, representatives of the 12 ger communities
included in the project have been consulted on the types of infrastructure upgrading
desired by the local communities and on the Municipality's proposals for meeting these
needs. The community's views were also solicited on a range of issues including site
conditions, drainage problems, pipeline routes and solid waste issues. To provide
information for selecting the project gers, a survey was initiated with the assistance of
community leaders on population incomes and expenditures as well as population
movement in and out of the 12 proposed project areas.
4.42  During implementation, the views of ger area representatives will continue to be
canvassed by the Municipality's local subadministrative agencies (khoroos) who will
participate in community development, and in the collection of ger infrastructure fund
charges. lIt is anticipated that the local community associations formed for project
preparation will be strengthened with the assistance of newly created nongovernmental
organizations, such as Women for Social Progress, which have taken an interest in ger
area activities and wish to facilitate community development and involvement in all
aspects of ger area upgrading. Measures proposed for maximum community participation
would be to:
(a)   identify community interest groups in ger areas such as women,
pensioners, single parents and youth, for the formation of community
councils bringing together district representatives;
(b)   organize meetings of community councils for general acceptance of water
vending kiosk locations, use of solid waste bins and duties of householders
for an efficient solid waste collection, initiation of community
management of facilities such as bathrooms and laundries;
(c)   introduce concepts such as small business cooperatives for such activities
as vegetable growing and ash waste recycling and public laundries; and
(d)   foster the idea that the infrastructure charge would be used for additional
infrastructure improvements and for related maintenance.
4.43  In addition, IDA  supervision  missions during  the first two  years of
implementation will include a sociologist to monitor local reactions to the project and
advise MUB on appropriate participatory approaches to program implementation.
L. ACCOUNTING AND AUDIT
4.44  To facilitate various market-oriented reforms, the Government required all public
enterprises, starting in 1996, to adopt a new enterprise accounting system, which is
consistent with internationally accepted accounting standards (IAAS). With financial
technical assistance under the project, USAG will convert to the new accounting system
and procedures to be consistent with IAAS by the end of 1998. The new accounting



- 40 -
system to be introduced will also set up a rigorous cost accounting system to enable
separate accounting of USAG's water supply and sewerage operations. This will increase
the transparency of costs and operations and will facilitate monitoring USAG's financial
performance and the adequacy of tariffs. Assurances were obtained during negotiations
that USAG will separate its water accounts from its sewerage accounts by December 31,
1998.
4.45  Assurances were obtained during negotiations that: (a) the project accounts,
including the Special Account and statements of expenditure, as well as USAG 'sfinancial
statements would be audited by independent auditors acceptable to IDA with a separate
opinion on SOEs; and (b) the audit reports would be sent to IDA for review within six
months of the close of each fiscal year. Given the project's TA to upgrade USAG's
accounting procedures and system and to implement an adequate internal control system
in the PMU, as well as the recent government efforts to improve its accounting and
auditing standards with ADB's support. The project will use the local auditing services
to the extent possible. This arrangement will not only reduce the cost of auditing, but
will help ensure that constant principles are applied to the IDA projects. As local
capacity and practices develop, the foreign accounting firm will be replaced by a local
auditor acceptable to the Association.
M. MONITORING, REPORTING AND SUPERVISION
4.46  The proposed project implementation schedule is shown in Annex 10, and project
performance and impact monitoring indicators are given in Annex 3. The format and
content of project progress reports and project impact monitoring indicators and
methodology would be discussed and confirmed during negotiations.   During
negotiations, assurances were obtained that MUB would prepare and furnish to IDA
biannual project implementation progress reports and annual financial reports including
the Special Account and statements of expenditures.
4.47  The Association's supervision of the project would focus on physical and
institutional progress, particularly enterprise restructuring and strengthening, besides
monitoring progress in achieving project objectives. Project supervision would require
about 75 staff-weeks over the life of the project, including 20 staff-weeks at headquarters
to review progress reports, procurement actions and reports and 40 staff-weeks in the
field. More intensive supervision would be required during the first three years of the
project when the technical assistance would be made operational and many procurement
contracts would be tendered, evaluated and awarded. Supervision would be provided by
environmental engineering and financial staff, as well as other specialists, e.g., a
sociologist, as needed.  During negotiations, assurances were obtained that a mid-
project review report would be submitted to IDA by October 30, 1999. The project
supervision plan is shown in Annex 11.

5. FINANCE
A. MUNICIPAL FINANCE
5.1   With more than 25 percent of Mongolia's total population, Ulaanbaatar had an
operating budget of Tug 23.9 billion in 1995, equivalent to 18 percent of the national
budget of Tug 131.3 billion. During the economic upheavals of the early 1990s, and
particularly in 1992, UB's budget shrank dramatically and, since then, has stabilized in
real terms. The budget is divided into the current budget for operating expenditures and
the capital budget for new investments. UB's operating budget has maintained a surplus
in the 1990s, except for a small deficit in 1995. The capital budget, however, is managed
separately from the operating budget and is financed by a combination of subsidies from
the central budget, municipal budget reserves, and off-budget sources such as a fixed
asset sales fund. UB has no outstanding debts and its nominal revenues have grown in
line with inflation since 1992. The expected introduction of a property tax on the recently
privatized apartments and UB's policy of increasing tariffs for urban services to recover
operating costs will help further consolidate its finances. A detailed description of the
municipality's financial situation and its projection are in Annex 12.
5.2   The proposed IDA Credit would be directly onlent from the Central Government
to USAG. UB, however, made a commitment to provide $1.26 million equivalent in
project counterpart funding, apart from the $0.8 million needed for taxes, and the already
earmarked Tug 100 million in the 1997 budget to carry out construction of two
connecting pipelines under the project. As the project's financial analysis shows that
USAG can generate sufficient cash flow to meet its debt service, the repayment of the
IDA Credit would not create an additional burden on municipal finance. Furthermore,
with the resumption of economic growth, the commencement of housing privatization
(which could provide a solid basis for charging adequate tariffs for various municipal
services) and the planned introduction of a variety of taxes and fees, the growth of
municipal revenues is likely to accelerate.  During negotiations, assurances were
obtained from UB that it will provide, directly or through USAG, the necessary
counterpart funds for execution of the project.
5.3   The AusAID  grant of $4.9 million equivalent will be passed on to the
Municipality. The investments supported by AusAID for the next five years amount to
50 percent of MUB's 1996 capital budget and will help relieve the burden on municipal
finance substantially.   Moreover, establishment of the Ger Area Infrastructure
Development Program, with its cost-recovery principle that encourages beneficiaries'
ownership of the facilities provided, and of separate account for the proceeds of the
charge, will help the municipality make sustainable infrastructure investments in the ger
areas.



- 42 -
B. USAG: FINANCIAL PERFORMANCE TO DATE
5.4   Mongolia has experienced significant hyperinflation since its breakaway from the
FSU in 1991. The price index rose from 100 in 1990 to 5,729 in 1996, with an average
annual inflation rate of 115.6 percent, while its currency was devalued from Tug 40/$1 in
1992 to Tug 695/$1 at the end of 1996. Given the hyperinflation, relatively slow and
less-than-sufficient revaluation of assets, and changes in the geographical scope of its
operation, USAG's historical data have inherent distortions, particularly in the balance
sheet. This analysis therefore summarizes USAG's recent performance, but because past
data should be approached with some caution, the analysis focuses on the future cash
flow and income statement data adjusted in 1996 constant money terms, which will
measure USAG's future cash generating and revenue-earning capacity.
5.5   Shrinking Revenues and Operations. USAG's financial situation in recent
years appears to have been sound based on its profit and loss statement. It has operated
with a cash surplus and small profit since the 1990s, except in 1994, and carries no
outstanding long-term debt. When ongoing hyperinflation pushed it into a small loss
amounting to 3 percent of total revenue in 1994, it increased its tariffs to allow a small
profit in 1995. This performance, which results from the Government's policy to keep
USAG's financial status near a break-even level by adjusting tariffs rather than increasing
efficiency, disguises many serious problems faced by USAG.
5.6   USAG's revenue has been shrinking at 38 percent a year in real terms since 1991
and its cash operating costs are also shrinking at 15 percent a year. This reflects tariff
adjustments that were lower than required to compensate for hyperinflation.  The
cumulative tariff adjustments for industrial users since 1991 were 3,571 percent,
compared to 5,729 percent cumulative inflation for the same period. Faced with the
shrinking revenues, USAG has been scaling down expenditures by forgoing critical
maintenance and rehabilitation investments, rather than improving its efficiency. The
lack of proper maintenance, if allowed to continue, will do irreparable damage to the
water supply infrastructure, which will incur unsustainably heavy costs in the near future.
5.7   USAG's current business practices also have a variety of inherent inefficiencies.
The most significant problem is its practice of charging apartment residents based on an
inadequate norm, which encourages high water wastage and inaction against leakage. In
addition, TANK operations and USAG's billing and collection system need urgent
improvement.
5.8   Billing and Collection. Billing and collection are currently carried out by the
kantors, which used to take a service fee from the collected revenues. Although
consumers diligently pay for water services, the kantors are usually delinquent in turning
over the revenues to USAG due to their own financial difficulties. This problem is
particularly acute in winter when the kantors face large heating and electricity bills. As a
result' USAG's accounts receivable are as high as 16 percent of sales in winter, dropping
to 2 or 3 percent in the summer. The size of the kantors' service fee has been determined



- 43 -
annually by negotiations between USAG and the kantors without an accurate basis to
price the kantors' services. However, the recent service fee level of over 20 percent of
revenues collected seems very high and reduces USAG's potential revenue. It is
recommended that USAG improve the current billing and collection system in the near
future, in conjunction with the planned privatization of kantors.
5.9   TANK  Operations.  Due to unsustainably high operating costs, TANK
operations have been losing money despite its exorbitant tariff charged to ger residents
and free water supply from USAG. TANK's unit operating cost of water in 1996
constant terns is Tug 535/m3, compared with Tug 33/m3 in the central city area.
Analysis of TANK operations shows that replacing most of TANK-delivered water with a
piped supply to the kiosks, as would be done under the project, can significantly reduce
operating costs while more than doubling the water supplied to ger residents to 20 lcd.
As indicated in Chapter 3, to reduce USAG's operating costs, TANK operations and staff
would be phased down as piped water supply is introduced in the project ger areas.
C. USAG: FINANCIAL PROJECTIONS
5.10  Tariffs. Following a recently introduced 60 percent increase in electricity prices,
which account for over 70 percent of USAG's operating costs, USAG raised its water
tariffs by a weighted average of 83 percent, effective from October 1996. The current
tariff structure is shown in Table 5.1. USAG has also streamlined its relationship with
the kantors by charging them directly and setting a 20 percent cap on the additional
charge kantors can impose on apartment dwellers.
TABLE 5.1: CURRENT TARIFF STRUCTURE
Industry/                Ger       Ger Tank
Tariff/Customer              Institutions  Apartment    Kiosks    Delivery
Previous (A), Tug                50          20         400         600
Revised (B), Tug                 97          34         400         600
Increase Rate (B-A)/A, percent   94          70           0           0
5.11  The introduction of metering under the proposed project will give USAG an
effective demand management tool to discourage water wastage. Following installation
of the project meters, USAG will introduce a "step tariff' by which it would charge a
much higher tariff for water use above a predetermined level (the "cutoff level"). The
cutoff level would be based on actual consumption rates after meter installation as well as
the desired, feasible consumption level. Under the project's sector improvement action
plan, USAG would prepare a program satisfactory to the Association for the introduction
of a step tariff; the program would be reviewed annually in consultation with the
Association during the last quarter of each fiscal year, starting in 1999.



- 44 -
5.12 Financial projections for USAG during 1997-2017 were undertaken based on
assumptions shown in Annex 13. Major assumptions include: the phase-out of the
TANK operation in ger areas, the phase-in of block metering, introduction of a special
maintenance fund, and annual tariff adjustments reflecting input cost increases. Detailed
financial statements are also given in Annex 13 and summarized in Table 5.2.
5.13  The projections indicate that USAG's current tariff, if maintained in real terms,
can generate sufficient operating income to cover all operating and maintenance costs
plus whichever is larger between depreciation and debt service during the project period.
This assumes an adequate pricing mechanism backed up by the installation of block
meters to reduce the uncharged volume of water. In a base case where average
consumption is assumed to fall from the present level of over 300 lcd to around 230 lcd,
the combination of expected cost savings from reduced water wastage and phase-out of
the expensive TANK truck supply could generate enough cash during the project period
to cover debt service on investments in the project's piped water supply to ger kiosks,
while equalizing the basic unit charge for water to ger with the unit charge to apartment
consumers.  These forecasts are based on conservative assumptions, and actual
consumption and future revenues of USAG may well be larger than the base case, barring
major changes in assumptions or consumer behavior. During negotiations, assurances
were obtained that beginning in USAG 's fiscal year commencing January 1, 1998, USAG
will take necessary measures, including adjustment of tariffs, to ensure that its annual
operating income will cover operating costs and the amount by which debt service
requirements exceed the provision for depreciation. To meet these measures, assurances
were also obtained that beginning in October 1997 and biannually each April and
October thereafter USAG would prepare and submit to its Board and IDA, annual
financial forecasts for the current and following years and the recommended tariff rate
and structure and timing of implementation.

6. PROJECT JUSTIFICATION
A. OVERVIEW
6.1   The proposed project, with its focus on ger area upgrading, will help ease the
social burden of structural reform and the country's transition to a market economy by
improving urban services and hygiene in the ger areas. The project would provide piped
water to about 169,000 people or 70 percent of the 12 ger areas supplied. About 75,000
of those beneficiaries would also gain other basic infrastructure under the project. Project
support and service improvements to the existing system would benefit all those now
served with a steadier supply at higher pressure. As the first IDA-supported urban
development project in Mongolia, it also introduces market-oriented provision and
management of urban services by providing an adequate cost recovery mechanism
combined with financial incentives for water conservation and operational efficiency.
Furthermore, an institutional development and reform action plan will reshape UB's
water supply and sanitation utility to function in a market economy in a financially and
environmentally equitable and sustainable manner.
B. EcONOMIc ANALYSIS
6.2   The project comprises three major components: water supply for UB's ger areas,
water supply to the central city area, and ger area upgrading. As each component has a
different type of economic benefit, the analysis is carried out by component. Quantitative
cost-benefit analysis, possible only for the two water supply components, focuses on the
incremental costs and benefits between the "with project" and "without project" cases,
based on the financial analysis. When necessary and appropriate, appropriate proxies and
shadow prices are used to measure economic benefits and costs that differ from financial
benefits and costs. Details of the economic analysis are given in Annex 14.
Ger Area Water Supply
6.3   In the ger areas, the benefits and costs have been quantified to the extent possible.
The major benefit is increased consumption of water to a level of 20 lcd, considered the
minimum requirement for health and hygiene, at a much lower price than before. The
economic benefit to ger residents is measured using the 1995 price of water as a proxy for
consumers' willingness to pay. An additional benefit is derived from the savings in
capital investments and recurrent costs to maintain a larger fleet of trucks and tanks,
owing to the introduction of a piped water supply to cover 70 percent of the ger area
population. This benefit is compared to the initial capital investment to provide water
supply to kiosks under the project. The resulting economic internal rate of return (EIRR)
is 19 percent. It should also be noted that the project's major benefits will accrue to ger
residents whose average income is estimated to be 70 percent of apartment dwellers'



- 47 -
incomes, and who include the urban poor. The additional consumption of potable water
will substantially enhance the level of health and hygiene in ger area, and easier access to
kiosks will also decrease work for ger area women and children who traditionally haul
water home. These qualitative benefits, albeit substantial, are not involved in the
quantitative analysis.
City Water Supply Improvements
6.4   In the central city water supply component, the benefits come from reduced
consumption of valuable resources rather than from incremental benefits or production.
In fact, the supply of water will decrease under the project. The "with project" case
assumes that, with the introduction of block metering and step tariffs, the wastage of
water will substantially decrease, requiring fewer resources to produce the water and
collect, treat and discharge wastewater. The benefits of treating less wastewater and
using fewer resources for water production are all captured as USAG handles both
operations. The resultant EIRR of 14.4 percent, however, understates the real benefits, as
the benefits of a more stable water supply with higher and more evenly distributed water
pressure backed by reserve capacity in reservoirs are difficult to quantify and are
therefore excluded from the analysis. Also omitted from the analysis is the unquantified
benefit of the enhanced efficiency and accountability derived from institutional
strengthening supported by project technical assistance.
6.5   The weighted average EIRR for the water supply component is estimated to be
15.9 percent, based on the above analysis. The real benefit of the proposed project would
be much larger, however, owing to its significant unquantified benefits. The very concept
of serving ger area residents equally with the apartment dwellers will also provide a basis
for similar future interventions for other types of municipal service.
Ger Area Infrastructure Improvements
6.6   Planned Ger improvement covers various subcomponents reflecting the priorities
of the Ger residents, generating various economic benefits. Most of these benefits,
though hard to quantify, are substantial and directly accrue to Ger residents. Provision of
total length of 10.8 km roads, a bridge, and 30.8 km all-weather footpaths with four
footbridges in areas with the most critical needs can help save substantial time for the
residents to haul coal and wood, other household goods as well as to fetch water. For
example, the maximum travel distance for 20 kg water fetching will be reduced from the
maximum of 700 m to 350 m, and the footpaths will help ease the labor of water fetching
usually carried out by women and children. The benefit from easier fuel hauling is no
less significant, considering the bulky nature of coal and wood and its position as the
second largest expenditure item in Ger area. The estimated time saved for water fetching
alone would amount to over 10 million hours/year for 86,000 people, similar saving is
expected coal, wood and other household supplies. Paved roads can also substantially
save the fuel cost and maintenance expenditure for delivery trucks and other vehicles, as
well as save the time for delivery. The economic rate of return for $1.4 million



- 48 -
investment for road construction is estimated to be over 40 percent. Construction and
operation of public bathhouses and laundries can improve the health of Ger residents.
Likewise, improvement in solid waste collection would improve the hygiene of Ger area
considerably. Also important are the benefits from the construction of the stormwater
drainage in flood-prone areas, which can help reduce the property damage as well as
avoid the loss of human lives. In addition to these direct benefits, overall infrastructure
improvements would motivate the voluntary investments for further improvements in
each individual plot by each household. Some of these benefits would be captured by the
increase in property prices, particularly in light of the forthcoming privatization of urban
land. To summarize, there would be substantial and direct economic benefits to Ger area
residents, although difficult to quantify, and this component will also play a catalytic role
to induce further improvements in the Ger area.
C. AFFORDABILITY
6.7   Water Supply Components. The combination of nearby good-quality water and
charging systems based on the norms of 150 lcd makes the water bill well affordable to
apartment dwellers, accounting for 0.44 percent of the average income. The project's
introduction of a penalizing step tariff is meant to discourage water wastage for those
consuming more than 230 lcd, but would not affect the affordability for normal
households who would consume less than 230 lcd. Special care will be taken to reduce
the unit cost of water in real terms to apartment dwellers in introducing the step tariff so
that the actual burden to the household consuming 230 lcd will remain the same as
before. As for ger consumers, with the dramatic downward adjustment of water charges
and growing incomes, the financial burden will slowly decrease from about 1 percent to
0.4 percent of the average income in 2000. The drop in price of ger water is so huge that
it can more than compensate for the anticipated twofold increase in per capita
consumption and still enhance the affordability to ger residents.
6.8   Ger Area Infrastructure Improvement.  To ensure that the ger area
infrastructure charge is affordable, it would be established as 1 percent of average ger
incomes. Annual revisions by MUB of the charge applied to a ger area scheduled for
improvement would reflect increases in improvement costs in the previous year, but
would not exceed increases in the previous year's ger incomes. Also, the additional
household payments for improvement costs would be fully offset by reductions in
household expenditure on piped water from kiosks at the greatly reduced unit prices to be
realigned during the project implementation period. Ger beneficiaries would also pay
user fees for the sanitation component (bathhouses and laundries). The level of the fee
would aim at full cost recovery of the operating costs, while taking into account ger
beneficiaries' capacity to pay for the services. As the bathhouses and laundries will be
connected to the ger piped water system, it is anticipated that the cost of water for
providing the services should be substantially reduced, thereby increasing the prospects
for cost recovery with affordable charges. Assurances were obtained from MUB during
negotiations that. (a) user charges for ger improvements would be established by March
31, 1998, and deposited in a Municipal Ger Area Infrastructure account, the sources of



- 49 -
which are to be usedfor additional ger infrastructure improvements or their maintenance
unless otherwise agreed with IDA; (b) by commencement of the improvement program it
will adopt a scheme acceptable to the Association protecting the interests of ger area
residents, contributing to the ger infrastructure program and presently without any land
title.
D. RISKS
6.9   As the first urban project in a rapidly changing transitional economy, the project
faces a number of risks. These risks have been identified and mitigating measures
developed to reduce the overall project risk to acceptable levels.
6.10 Project Management. With MUB's thin capacity for project management and
relatively little exposure to IDA's procurement procedures, the project faces the risk of
implementation delays. However, AusAID-funded technical assistance has helped the
Municipality prepare major procurement packages for the first and second project years,
amounting to 25 percent of all project procurement. The project also allocates about $0.7
million for technical assistance in project implementation to ensure the availability of
appropriate expertise. In addition, a government official from MID, who is seconded to
the Bank Group for a year, will work full-time for the project during its implementation.
Frequent IDA supervision of the project during the first two years of implementation
would also provide strong startup support.
6.11  Institutional Risk. A relatively high degree of risk is inherent in enterprise
restructuring and commercialization in any transition economy.   The proposed
restructuring and institutional change in USAG is no exception. However, actions taken
during project preparation and planned for the implementation period should offset part
of this risk. These include: (a) the firm commitment of municipal authorities at the
highest levels to USAG's commercial reorientation and the reforms to be introduced;
(b) USAG staffs growing understanding of the proposed changes as a result of
discussions held during project preparation; (c) technical assistance and training to be
provided under the project to facilitate key reforms; and (d) the phased approach to
reorganization taken under USAG's Institutional Development Action Plan and its
inclusion of measures to reduce the pain of restructuring, such as staff downsizing
through attrition and retraining of redundant staff for new positions. These measures,
coupled with the potential demonstration effect to other state-owned enterprises of
USAG's commercialization, make this risk acceptable.
6.12  Although ongoing and expected market-oriented reforms in the municipality
would act to underpin project reform, possible development in some parts of the city and
the potential privatization of land could diminish or erase the benefits of the project's ger
infrastructure improvements to poor ger residents.
6.13  Cost Recovery. USAG's cost recovery will depend on MUB's willingness to
make timely and adequate adjustments to the water supply tariff in line with cost
increases. While both the central and municipal governments have made cost recovery
for municipal services a central feature of their urban strategies, the performance of the



- 50-
economy-which cannot be predicted with any certainty-will strongly affect the
announced political commitment. As part of the Sector Improvement Action Plan,
USAG and MUB have agreed to undertake biannual reviews of USAG's two year
financial projections with the recommended tariff and structure to meet the financial
covenant (para. 5.13).  In ger areas, the level of cost recovery for the proposed
Infrastructure Account as well as the fee for public baths and other facilities would be
decided within affordability limits and would be implemented with active participation of
the local community.
6.14 Consumer Response to Block Metering. Although block metering would not be
as effective an incentive as individual metering to discourage waste, potential consumer
resistance to decreasing wastage will be countered by the introduction of a penalizing
step-tariff for excessive consumption. This will be complemented by investment in
fixture repairs, vigorous public awareness program to promote water conservation, and
the planned introduction of a new system for collecting housing management fees, which
will group a small number of apartments in one collection unit.
6.15  Technical Risks. The risks involved in cold climate engineering have been
mitigated by enlisting the advice of a consultant with extensive Alaskan experience to
ensure appropriate project designs. The engineering alternatives selected for the project
are already in use on other schemes in more severe climactic conditions, which use the
technology incorporated in the project design.
E. SENSITIVITY TEST
6.16  A test to measure the sensitivity of EIRR of the water supply component was
carried out for major variables, including different level of water consumption by ger
residents as well as apartment residents. If the average water consumption by ger
residents turns out to be 15 lcd and 25 lcd, rather than assumed 20 lcd, the resultant EIRR
would change to 13.2 and 17.2 percent, respectively. Likewise, if the average apartment
resident consumes 200 lcd and 250 lcd of water instead of 230 lcd in the base case, the
EIRR would be 16.1 and 13.8 percent, respectively. This is consistent with the analysis,
as the major sources of economic benefits are increased welfare in ger area and the
savings of valuable resources from the reduction of waste in apartments. Thus, even
under the unfavorable scenarios of lower water consumption in ger area or less than
expected level of wastage reduction in apartments, the project remains viable. It is also
important to note that, under both scenarios, the effect on the financial viability of the
project is negligible.



- 51 -

- 55 -                     ANNEX I
ANNEX 1: THE ULAANBAATAR WATER SUPPLY SYSTEM
INTRODUCTION
1.    Prior to 1950, Ulaanbaatar (UB) had relatively few inhabitants who lived
exclusively in gers and took their water from the Tuul River. But with significant
population growth, the tanking of water became necessary in 1950, with deliveries made
directly to family plots. Wells were also used but, by 1965, they were closed due to
pollution and the system of water sales kiosks was established.
2.    Also from about 1958, the construction of a piped water reticulation system was
established in the city's formally developed area and that system has been extended and
improved since then. The water supplied to the system is primarily from aquifers and is
obtained from wellfields with numbers of boreholes, each with its submersible pump.
Once lifted from the boreholes, the water is chlorinated before being pumped to supply.
These networks directly supply the formally developed areas of the city, while the peri-
urban areas still rely on the operation of a road tanker fleet that obtains most of its supply
via filling stations connected to the water mains.
3.    The present supply system suffers from aging assets, intermittent supplies and
lack of pressure in the pipelines, with the consequent risk of contamination.
WATER DEMAND
4.    In 1995, water demand in UB totaled about 161,935 m3/d, of which 121,532 m3/d
from apartments (296,420 persons at 410 lcd), 38,159 m3/d from industry, and 2.244 m3 /d
from ger residents (224,443 persons at 10 lcd). UB's 74,000 apartments with hot and
cold running water housed about 323,000 in 1995, of whom 296,420 were supplied from
the municipal water supply network.
WATER SUPPLY SYSTEM
5.    Supply Sources. There are four existing water supply sources called the Upper,
Central, Industrial and Meat Complex. They all extract groundwater from aquifers along
the Tuul River. The groundwater source capacity has been investigated and found to
have potential for future development.
6.    Upper Source is located about 45 km upstream of UB. Water is collected from 39
wells, and intake pumps deliver it to 2/1,000 m3 storage reservoirs (Total Water Level,
TWL, 1,425 m). The water is pumped over a ridge to the "X" reservoir (not yet
commissioned) and the city. There are six pumps at the pump station, each with a
nominal capacity of 1,000 m3/hr. The pipeline has a design capacity of 72,000 m3/d, but



- 56-                      ANNEX I
is currently delivering only 24,000 m3/d, with delivery pressure much less than expected.
Only 1.2 pumps (operating 20 hr/d) are needed to deliver the current production, so there
is potential to substantially increase output from the Upper Source. The majority of flow
from the Upper Source pipeline is proposed to go to the Northeast Reservoir (2/6,000 m3
tanks with a TWL of 1,387 m), which is not yet in service.
7.    Central Source is located on the Tuul River near the city center. Water is
collected from 70 wells and delivered to 10,000 m3 reservoirs (6,000 m3, 3,000 m3, 2/500
mi3). Its design capacity is 97,000 m 3/d, and it is presently delivering about 90,000 m3/d
to the city network. The main delivery pipeline comprises 3/600 mm diameter mild steel
mains that direct flow into the network and toward the Tasgan Reservoir (3/6,000 m3
tanks) with a TWL of 11,335 m.
8.    Industrial Source is located downstream of the Central Source and includes 16
wells with intake pumps that deliver to 2/2,000 mi3 storage reservoirs. The pump station
contains four pumps with nominal capacities of 2/200 and 2/250 m3/hr. The delivery
mains to the city network and industrial area are 500, 3/400 and 300 mm diameter mild
steel mains. The design capacity is 43,000 m 3/day and it is now delivering about 22,000
m3/d, which means that all four pumps are operating 24 hr/d.
9.    Meat Complex Source is located downstream of the Industrial source and includes
8 wells that deliver water to 2/2,000 mi3 storage. The pump station has four pumps with
nominal capacities of 2/160 and 2/250 m3/hr. The delivery mains to the city network and
meat processing area are 2,400 and 2/250 mm diameter mild steel mains that now deliver
about 16,000 m'3/d of flow to the city. This flow will be directed to the Northwest
reservoir (2/3,000 m3 tanks with TWL of 1,347 m) when it comes into service. All
pumps are operating 20 hr/d, so there are no standby pumps.
10.   Reservoirs: Utilization of Unused Capacity in the Proposed Project. There
are five existing reservoirs in the system on the hills north and east of UB. They are not
linked to each other, or fully utilized to provide adequate system storage or gravity
system pressure. USAG and MUB have indicated the inadequate use of the reservoirs is
due to inadequate information about their level of filling, and that a telemetric system
would assist in an integrated use of all reservoirs. Nor, with the exception of the
easternmost (Northeast) reservoir, are they high enough to service by gravity the large ger
population in the large valley north of UB. To a large extent, pressure is maintained in
the network by direct pumping from the well-head sources. However, many areas suffer
from inadequate water supply and pressure, and facilities serving high-rise apartment
blocks are not equipped to boost-pump water to the higher floors in nine-story buildings.
11.   The total water storage volume available is 48,000 mi3, of which only 24,000 m 3 is
currently used. Fifty percent of constructed service storage remains a dormant asset due
to weak points (weak valves and pipe segments), particularly in the northeastern section
of the deteriorated city network, which would not withstand the higher pressures caused
by putting the reservoirs in service. While pipe segments are designed for working



- 57 -                     ANNEX 1
pressure of 100 to 120 m, in practice they break at about 60 to 80 m head. The proposed
project would therefore rehabilitate the transmission system and commission the
reservoirs in order to increase pressure in the system, provide a continuous water supply
and make more water available to users.
12.   The "X" Reservoir. The unused "X" reservoir was constructed to operate as a
break pressure tank to limit pressures into the city network, transmission system
weaknesses delayed reservoir commission. The reservoir comprises 2/3,000 m3 tanks
with TWL of 1,430 m. With the reservoir, the hydraulic head developed at the location
where the main transmission main enters the city is around 1,385 m, whereas current
pressures are about 1,350 m. With this available head, the pressures are enough to supply
flows by gravity directly to the Northeast Reservoir, also now unused. This would allow
the Northeast Reservoir, commanding pressures from an elevation of 1,382 m, to function
as a supply reservoir for the Hailast Reservoir to be constructed under the proposed
project.
13.   "3/4" and Northeast Reservoirs. The "3/4" Reservoir would be mobilized to
serve both as a supply reservoir for the immediate city network and to fill up the two
adjacent lower reservoirs, the Northwestern and the Tasgan. As the highest of the three
reservoirs, the "3/4" can also be used to supplement the other two reservoirs and thereby
eliminate the need for the pumping now carried out from the Tasgan reservoir to the city
network, as well as to fill the Northwest reservoir. A 500 mm diameter northern link
would also provide additional filling capacity into the Northeast reservoir, for which
supply would be to both the city network and to the ger reservoir at Hailast.
14.   Northwestern Reservoir. The project's southern link pipeline will provide a
direct connection to the Northwestern Reservoir from the upper source.
15.   Raw Water Pipelines. There are about 98 km of unlined mild steel pipelines
conveying the raw water from the well intake pumps to the source storages.
16.   Water Quality. A JICA study indicates that the water generally complies with
Mongolian and World Health Organization standards, although levels of chrome,
manganese and fluoride occasionally exceed desirable levels in one of the aquifers close
to the industrial zone.
17.   Distribution System. Each of the four main water source works delivers water to
its supply system. These systems are interconnected at strategic locations but do not
incorporate either flow or pressure control. The pipes are of steel or cast iron and range
in diameter from 100 mm to 800 mm. Generally, pipes are laid at a depth of 3 to 3.5 m.
The system was built from 1958 to 1990 and extends some 240 km.
18.   The delivery of water to the distribution networks is by pumping: first from the
ground reservoirs at the source works and thereafter either from USAG's booster stations
or from the Central Heating Point (CTP) stations or booster pumps at individual
buildings. The supply of water therefore depends on the continued availability of



- 58 -                     ANNEX I
electrical power at various locations around the city, and since this is intermittent, so too
is the availability of water. When power is off, the pressure in the water mains is lost and
vacuum conditions develop, thus drawing in air to the pipework. Where the integrity of
underground pipes is poor, this negative pressure can draw groundwater into the system,
thereby creating contamination.
19.   Except for sections supplied by the "Tasgan" and "3/4" reservoirs, the water
supply network "floats" on the pumping plant that has to respond to diurnal changes in
demand. This method of operations is inefficient and provides a poor level o f service.
20.   Pipe Materials Used and Cold Climate Effects. Pipes are either steel or cast
iron, with no internal lining or protection. The outside of some pipe is coated with
bitumen or wrapped with asphalt sheets. Lack of internal protection leads to rust stains,
one of the main causes of discoloration in the water supply. Trench depths are usually 2
to 3 m, and there appears to be a lack of control during installation of the trenching
methods and provision of adequate bedding and compacting of materials. Pipe joints are
generally caulked and without adequate maintenance may have deteriorated and be a
major cause of the pipelines not withstanding high pressures. Pressure above about 60 to
80 m results in significant leakages into pipe and appurtenances. A common practice to
prevent the pipelines from freezing is to install cold water pipes in the same trench as
heating or hot water pipes, but this is limited to installations at and downstream of the
CTPs.



- 59 -                     ANNEX2
ANNEX 2: DETAILED PROJECT DESCRIPTION
WATER SUPPLY INVESTMENTS
1.    This component deals with three different aspects of the system  and its
operations:
(a)   strengthening of the existing network and removal of bottlenecks on the
primary distribution network;
(b)   the construction of additional distribution facilities to improve the service
in ger areas and to reduce the access cost to users, and;
(c)   the implementation of a water wastage reduction program for apartment
areas aimed at bringing overall consumption levels, which are now
inordinately high and reflect major losses to leakage, in line with more
typical levels through control by metering and replacement of leaking
facilities.
2.    Strengthening of the existing network is composed of the following:
(a)   continuation and completion of a transmission loop around the main areas
of the city, with the supply and laying of pipelines for the northern
transmission main (3.0 km of 300 mm diameter), linking between the
reservoirs at Northwest, 3/4 district, Tasgan and Northeast locations
(18.0 km of 500/400/300 mm diameter) and the southern links for the
city's network main (12 km of 400 mm diameter);
(b)   rehabilitation and commissioning of the Northeast and X reservoirs,
coupled with installation of a telemetry system intended to optimize use of
the reservoirs;
(c)   the repair and replacement of existing valves and installation of regulating
valves to increase pressures on secondary networks presently ill served;
and
(d)   provision of equipment and spares for the workshop and laboratory.
3.    Construction of Additional Distribution Facilities. This would improve the
supply of water to ger areas and reduce the cost of water to the local inhabitants. The
component would develop reservoirs and networks for 12 of the city's existing 18 ger
areas. The project areas selected are, unlike the other six areas, not scheduled for urban



-60-                      ANNEX 2
redevelopment or dissolution due to permafrost. The project ger areas are mostly in the
northeast and northwest fringes of the formally built-up districts and include the areas of
Gandan, Denjin, Hailast, Chingeltei, Shar Had, Dambadarjaa, Dari Ekh, Tolgoit, Naran,
Baiyankoshuu, Nisek and Yarmag. Works would comprises the following:
(a)   about 93 km of specially insulated pipes for installation as the primary
network in ger areas, with risers to the public water kiosks serving the
population;
(b)   supply and laying of two transmission pipelines (9.3 km of 300/500 mm
diameter) and 2.7 km of 500 mm diameter) linking the ger distribution
system to the existing city network;
(c)   construction of three semiburied reservoirs at Hailast, Yarmag and Shar
Had, with a total capacity of 2,500 m3;
(d)   drilling of two boreholes at Yarmag and Baiyankoshuu to supply areas that
are more economically served through independent groundwater supply;
and
(e)   construction of 25 new water kiosks and rehabilitation of 110 old ones
serving the 12 ger areas.
4.    The water wastage reduction program comprises the following investments:
(a)   supply and installation of about 22,000 cold and hot water meters for
apartment building cores;
(b)   supply of fixtures and piping for internal use in apartments and buildings;
and
(c)   provision of meter testing and repair facilities.
5.    Ger Upgrading Improvement Program.
(a)   Construction of a program of drains, culverts and flood protection walls
and levees in Denjin, Chingeltei and Dambadarjaa totaling about:
* 2.6 km of concrete sided and bottomed drains, and 2.2 km levees;
*  construction of 28.1 km, 1-1.2 m wide footpaths;
* 10.8 km of paved and gravel roads in all five ger areas selected under
this first upgrading program;
(b)   Construction of four footbridges and a roadbridge in the areas of
Chingeltei and Dambadarjaa; and



-61-                         ANNEX 2
*  manufacture and installation of some 650 3 m3 bins for refuse
collection in all five ger areas;
(c)    Supply of several refuse collection trucks, compactors and equipment to
improve the collection along ger tracks; and
(d)   Construction and installation or repair of one set of bathhouse and or
laundry facilities in each of the five ger areas.

ANNEX 3: PROJECT PERFORMANCE AND IMPACT
INDICATORS
1. Poverty Alleviation
* Number of water kiosks served by a piped supply
*  Ratio of the unit price of water to ger areas and to apartments to become one
by end of project period
* Number of ger plots with access to footpath/road within 50 meters
* Per capita ger consumption of water
* Growth of Ger Infrastructure Revenues
2. Efficiency Enhancement
* Decline in per capita water consumption in apartment buildings
* Collection rates of water revenues for apartments and institutions/industries
- Amount of water delivered by truck
- Population served by USAG water supply to total number of USAG staff
* Ratio of revenue to number of USAG staff
