5 Finance and Accounts Expert                          4.5
Subtotal                                         32.0
6 Computers and Related Equipment                                  25,000
7 Vehicles                                                         25,000
8 Office Support and Interpreters                     30.0         45,000
Subtotal                                                      95,000
C. MAINTENANCE DEVELOPMENT OF USAGE-TERMS OF REFERENCE
Background
40.   The Government of Mongolia has applied to the International Development
Association (IDA) for a Credit amounting to $16.1 million which is to be onlent to the
Water and Wastewater Company USAG in Ulaanbaatar to support program of
investments to improve and expand its water and wastewater services. An equally
important part of the project is an institutional development of USAG including
maintenance improvement component. For this purpose a technical assistance of a
specialist-consultant is to be provided to help USAG in developing and implementing a
proper maintenance techniques and procedures.
41.   Under 1991 Public Enterprise Law, USAG as hitherto existing a municipality arm
is predicted to be converted into a limited liability company with the Municipality of UB



- 92 -                    ANNEX6
as the Owner. With such given autonomy, USAG is expected to operate more efficiently
during and after implementation of the institutional strengthening program.
42.   USAG operates a water supply system that currently includes 300 km of water
pipelines and well fields of some 110 wells and pump stations, while the sewerage system
has an 80 km network that feeds into a wastewater treatment plant before discharge. In
addition, USAG provides water supply for the ger areas through 262 public water kiosks,
receiving water from a fleet of about 50 water tanker trucks.
43.   Due to the insufficient tariff increases authorized by the Municipality in the past,
USAG has lacked adequate revenue to carry out system maintenance. It currently has no
regular maintenance procedures, and maintenance is usually performed only when
absolutely necessary. Now, with the revenues expected from a substantial tariff increase
in October 1996, the budged for maintenance will be increased by 10 percent annually in
real terms and should become available for a much stepped-up maintenance program.
According to this budget agreed with IDA and kept over the project years 1998-2001, all
maintenance expenditure shall be covered.
Objectives
44.   The main objective of this consultant assignment is to assist USAG in planning,
designing and introducing more efficient maintenance procedures to enhance USAG
capability and efficiency, to improve quality of provided services, and to maintain the
value of its own assets. The TA will be accompanied by training of USAG staff.
Scope of Works
45.   Assessment of Existing Situation. As a basis for any decision making, a good
understanding of the present condition of the water and sewage network, treatment
stations, reservoirs, buildings and other tangible assets is essential. Therefore, the
program should begin with the survey of physical condition of those facilities and the
record of their life (repairing, replacement, modernization). Available information,
including maps, damages record and statistics, a kind of material and an age of pipelines
will be very useful.
46.   As a second step, the review of the current maintenance procedures, including
planning, reporting, execution, technical quality standards, status of spare parts and a
survey of the existing maintenance equipment, should be done.
47.  In carrying out these assessments, a thorough analysis of the information already
available to USAG should be done. Further field examinations, analysis and presentation
of information would be carried out in accordance with a plan of action agreed with
USAG.
48.   Elaboration of Prioritized Maintenance Plan. Information gained during the
preliminary assessment (noted in above paragraphs) will be used to identify and analyze



- 93 -                    ANNEX6
investment interventions needed as a priority. This will involve the list of actions
including repairing or replacement of facilities and renewing buildings. Special emphasis
should be given to the identification of win-win solution. For instance, the replacement
of existing pumps with more energy efficient ones, or the replacement of old corroded
pipes may reduce water contamination and water leakage.  The prepared list of
maintenance investment should be ranked in accordance with definition of priorities.
49.   Preparation of Detailed Annual Maintenance Plans.  Based on prepared
prioritized maintenance plan described above, the Consultant should assist USAG in
preparing specific annual maintenance plans based on available budget, which has to be
submitted to the Governing Board by October 15 of each year, except for 1998 when it
would be by March 15, 1998. The first year plan may need to focus on simple tasks
enhancing the work enviromnent.
50.   Developing Suitable Maintenance Procedures. The Consultant shall assist
USAG in developing a routine maintenance procedure based on:
* analysis of age, depreciation value and technical quality standard of existing
facilities (network, buildings, equipment)
a analysis of past record of damages and its statistic
* cost estimation of maintenance activity including investment
51.   After finishing the TA program provided by the Consultant, USAG staff should
be able to manage the routine maintenance procedure including the following tasks:
n preparation of annual maintenance plans, including ranking in accordance with
definition of priorities and pricing with using software tools
* operating with advance maintenance equipment for leakage detection, water
meters calibration and installation, sewer cleaning equipment operation
* planning water network areas designated for day to day investigation due to
water leakage prevention
* preparation bidding procedures for goods purchasing
* preparing and defining inventory listing for store of spares
* preparing reports on day-to-day maintenance
52.   Implementation Assistance.  The Consultant will assist USAG  in the
implementation of the up-to-date maintenance techniques including:
* implementation of high quality standard materials, fittings and devices



-94 -                     ANNEX6
* implementation of modern repairing technologies
* implementation of advance method of daily routine maintenance activities
(water leakage detection)
The "Coaching" Role of the Consultant
53.   The main role of the Consultant will be to provide organization, know how
guidance and general facilitation of maintenance work is done day-to-day by USAG staff.
All Maintenance Department staff subordinated to the Chief Engineer are expected to be
fiully involved for successful final result of this job training. A contract agreement for the
consultant services should carefully define counterpart input and involvement in to this
project for assuring its prospective expected benefits.
54.   The Consultant may also assist in defining and acquiring special equipment (pipe
locators, plugs for closing off sewers, sewer cleaning equipment , meter calibration
equipment ) to be paid under IDA funding.
Qualification of the Consultant
55.   The Consultant should be experienced in maintenance procedure and asset
management and should fulfill the following conditions:
* work in a Public Utility for at least 10 years ( a recently retired person would
be acceptable);
 experience in maintenance workshop;
* education: technician or engineer; and
* Russian language would be a definite plus.
Timing and Budget
56.   The TA will require the services of a Consultant with a total of 9 man-months of
foreign consultant  input.  Total budget is estimated to be $150,000 including
contingencies for a study tour to a public utility for three people of the maintenance
department. It is expected, that whole training program and implementation assistance
will start since January 1998 and will be finished in December 2000. The detailed
program will be agreed with USAG after completion of the phase on the existing
situation phase.
Schedule and Reporting
57.   The technical assistance is to be provided under this TOR during a period of 3
years. Inputs are expected to be heavier at the beginning of the assignment, tapering off



- 95 -                    ANNEX6
as the USAG maintenance department staff takes over the responsibility for preparing and
implementing yearly maintenance schedules.
58.   The Consultant will submit brief reports after each visits in USAG according to
his agreed schedule of work. Reports prepared in the English language will summarize:
(a) progress achieved; (b) difficulties encountered; (c) action expected by USAG services;
and (d) issues to be resolved. Six copies of each report (prepared in Mongolian by the
PMU) will be dispatched to USAG (4 copies to the General Manager and his Deputies,
two to the members of the Board) and two copies (in English) to IDA in Washington.
Materials, Data and Support Services
59.   The Consultant, when providing technical assistance, will work in the offices of
the USAG Maintenance Departments and will be provided with sufficient furnished
office accommodation at no cost to the Consultant. USAG commits itself to provide all
necessary and available data at no cost to the Consultant.
60.   Computers and related office equipment, software and vehicles, which are already
available in the PMU office, would be also made available to the Consultant.
61.   The PMU will provide appropriate support staff to work with the Consultant
(especially interpreters), and will arrange introductions to other agencies if required.
62.   Under the Contract Agreement for consultant services, the Consultant's
remuneration will cover all payment costs plus all costs in connection with international
travel, accommodation and subsistence.
63.   The Consultant will be reimbursed, at cost, for any expenses reasonably incurred
in connection with telecommunications, courier services and printing, and local travel
related to the provision of these consultancy services.
D. AN URBAN MANAGEMENT AND GER/CITY DEVELOPMENT PLANNING AND
STRATEGY STUDY-TERMS OF REFERENCE
Background
64.   The current Master Plan was prepared in 1986, before the massive changes in
Mongolia's political and economic situation forced the curtailment of the apartment
building program. In the longer run, the inefficient, current Ger layout in long lanes and
large (400-700 m 2) plots, without reservations for community open space and facilities,
or a functional road hierarchy, will need to be redeveloped or reshaped, at least in some
areas.
65.   The Master Plan needs to be revised to incorporate the current situation, in which
the majority of the Ger areas are likely to develop into permanent residential areas. The
form of tenure to be provided to Ger residents under the ongoing (in 1997) program to



- 96 -                      ANNEX6
privatize housing and land should take into account future needs to restructure the
infrastructure, reassemble or redefine the allowed land use on a plot or plots in a more
efficient configuration, which will also be sensitive to constraints on the availability of
funds for infrastructure investments and the affordability of services and plots to existing
and future ger residents, particularly in low-income groups.
66.   The apartment housing estates are clustered near the city center or along the main
roads of UB and are relatively well-served by public transport-the favored mode up to
1990. (Private car use and ownership has been growing rapidly since then.) The
apartment units have individual cold and hot water, toilets and bathrooms, conventional
piped sewage collection, on-site and off-site underground, piped stormwater drainage,
electricity and hot water heating and lifts in higher buildings. USAG provides the cold
water for hot and cold water supply in apartments. The hot water is generated in CTPs in
the housing sites. Heating hot water and electricity for the apartments (and institutional
buildings) are generated in central city plants serving the whole city. Apartment rents
and utility charges have until recently been heavily subsidized.
67.   Due to economic and political changes following the withdrawal of the FSU,
apartment building in the public sector has practically ceased and the small amount of
private construction is focused on higher-income housing. UB's housing future for the
majority of the population appears to lie in Ger area development.
68.   The existing Ger area are mostly on the outlying hills and valleys to the north,
northwest and northeast of UB and 2-12 kilometers from UB's economic activity.
69.   Families interested in obtaining a Ger plot apply to local Khoroo offices (city
subdistricts, the smallest unit of city administration). Plots of 300-500 m are located and
staked out by a city surveyor. Most new residents obtain plots this way, although there is
an increasingly active city real estate market. Only 10 percent of the plots are estimated
to be occupied by unregistered settlers.
70.   As all land was owned by government and the semidesert land is open,
uncultivated, though hilly and sometimes deeply-ravined, it is amenable to rapid Ger
development with few legal or natural constraints-except for water supply and possibly
heating fuel.
71.   Ger are laid out in simple, relatively long and narrow, rectilinear plots with long,
wide public rights-of-way between rows of plots. Plot-holders construct wooden fences
around their plots and have constructed a mixture of Ger (a circular, demountable wooden
tent with a felt and canvas cover), wooden frame and masonry structures. Because of
large plot sizes and the configuration of the infrastructure, Ger densities of 110/ha (with
extremes of 45 and 200 in the selected areas) are quite low. On the other hand, the
existing layouts lack provisions for cornmunity services and a structure that is amenable
to cost-effective, affordable and higher-density future development.



-97-                       ANNEX6
72.   Thus, a Study is needed of the financial, planning, land use and management tools
and costs and a strategy for Ger redevelopment/reshaping in an efficient, manageable,
equitable and affordable manner-an Urban Management And Ger/City Development
Planning and Strategy Study.
Urban Planning and Management Issues
73.   The key issues to be addressed in the Study, which would be with particular
reference to Ger areas in Ulaanbaatar City, and which are within the Scope of Work of
this Study are discussed below.
74.   Guidance Regarding Ger/City Redevelopment/Reshaping and Expansion.
The issues to be addressed in the Study, are:
(a)   the costs and benefits to households and the city of densification versus
expansion to meet city development and growth requirements;
(b)   the direction(s) of growth and location of services with regard to natural
constraints and sensitivities (e.g., permafrost, aquifers, water pressure) and
realistic availability of financial resources for service investments,
operations and maintenance;
(c)   open space/recreation requirements and the affordability of housing and
settlements, particularly to lower-income households; and
(d)   practical and effective instruments to guide growth, including assessment
of government's capacity and instruments for guiding/regulating land use
and development and managing the location, standards and costs of roads,
water supply, electricity supply, industrial estates, markets, transport and
other infrastructure.
75.   Management and Provisions for City Services (not covered adequately by other
contemporary studies, such as water supply and distribution). These would include
analysis and recommendations regarding:
(a)   transport infrastructure (railroads, roads, bridges, foot/cycle paths;)
(b)   transport modes (train, tram, bus, paratransit, auto, cycle, walk);
(c)   drainage;
(d)   sanitation;
(e)   health and education services; and
(f)   community and recreation services.



-98 -                      ANNEX6
76.   Institutional  Requirements.    These  would  include  analysis  and
recommendations regarding:
(a)   responsibilities for services (central/local government, pubic or private
sector);
(b)   legal and regulatory environment for managing land development and
urban services;
(c)   intersectoral coordination, planning and budgeting;
(d)   performance monitoring and public disclosure; and
(e)   stakeholder participation in planning and management
77.   The System and Base for Financing Urban Services. This would include
analysis and recommendations regarding:
(a)   central, local government and private responsibilities for investment and
operations and maintenance of specific urban services;
(b)   expenditure requirements and revenue base for specific services;
(c)   municipal tax and revenue base and potential for expanding the tax base
from, inter alia, businesses, entertainment and property development and
sales transactions (real estate);
(d)   central government grants and loans system for local services; and
(e)   municipal accounting and financial management.
Objectives
78.   The objectives of the Study, with special reference to new and existing ger and
development areas are:
(a)   to develop physical, institutional and financial strategies and mechanisms
to guide UB's future development, particularly in existing and new Ger
areas, with a view to the affordability of land to lower-income groups;
(b)   to strengthen the capabilities of UB's municipality, community and private
sectors to plan and manage the city on an ongoing basis.
Scope of Work
79.   The scope of work would be organized under three heads:
(a)   Growth Strategy and Services Planning;



-99 -                       ANNEX6
(b)   Legal and Institutional Strengthening; and
(c)   Financial Management Planning.
80.   The Study will be largely based on existing information/data, much of which is
available from project feasibility and engineering studies, during Phases 1 and 2, for the
USIP. "Urban services" for the purpose of the Study refers to all infrastructure and
equipment in UB, including railroads, roads and bridges, foot/cycle paths, drainage,
sewerage, sanitation, water supply and solid waste management, excluding detailed
analyses of gas, coal and electricity, which are provided by parastatal or companies.
Social services to be included in the Study are: health, education, community facilities,
recreation, police and fire services and government.
81.   Where appropriate, the Study will distinguish between: (a) investment in physical
facilities; (b) their operation and maintenance costs (O&M); (c) investment and operating
costs of social services and solid waste management; and (d) administration of
government. MUB considers that analysis of water supply and solid waste will not need
to be undertaken under the Study since these sectors have been analyzed during the
preparation of the USIP. However, related policies, plans and financing arrangements for
these sectors should be included in the Study's strategic plan and the Study's institutional
and financial proposals.
82.   The scope of work would be organized under three heads, with their associated
tasks, as follows:
(a)    Growth Strategy and Services Planning. This Study activity would
involve:
(i)   analyzing the economic and demographic base and trends in the
UB region in yearly increments over the next 20 years;
(ii)    preparing prototype layouts of existing and proposed new Ger
areas including social, health, education and local commercial
facilities, with alternative levels of service for access (roads and
footpaths, water supply and sanitation and alternative plot sizes
(the latter to take into account differences in densities related to
location) and assessing the affordability of plots to households in
various income groups, using realistic assumptions about the cost
and availability of capital and credit and permissible/recommended
provisions for land use.
(iii)   estimating of costs of the alternatives and cost per plot and per
family, excluding costs of social, educational and commercial
areas;



-100 -                        ANNEX6
(iv)    estimating  family  affordability to purchase plots/housing  at
alternative densities developed from the cost estimates (above);
(v)    estimating (order of magnitude) of land requirements and major
public infrastructure requirements and major commercial,
industrial and transport land requirements and their cost, using
population growth rates and alternative settlement densities
prepared above (e.g., for roads, sewerage, water supply and
drainage)-for five-year intervals over 20 years, separating
requirements to meet the backlog of services from requirements to
meet new growth;
(vi)    analyzing natural environmental systems and resources of the UB
region, identification of highly sensitive areas where development
should be excluded, and classification of remaining land types
according to their suitability for ger/future housing development in
different land uses;
(vii)   identifying existing ambient water and air quality levels, major
existing sources of water and air pollution and solid and toxic
waste sources, estimation of expected increases in pollution levels
due to the recommended development strategy and measures
required to curb pollution from major sources;
(viii)   identifying funds expected to be available for capital investment in
infrastructure (from central government grants, local government's
own revenues including revenues generated by its service agencies,
loans, external grants and private capital in yearly increments over
the next 10 years;
(ix)    matching costs of alternative development densities, infrastructure
standards and costs, for local area and macro infrastructure,
projected growth rates and environmental constraints (above) with
estimated affordable financing available to households (above) and
the public and private sectors, to prepare policies on:
*  densities, service levels and spatial layouts, regulations to plot
subdivision for Ger development to respond to private and
market influences;
*  potential for area housing  development, apartments and
commercial development by the formal sector in existing and
proposed future ger areas;
*  design and performance standards and service levels for macro
and micro infrastructure;



-101-                       ANNEX6
(x)    selecting best alternative options for development on the basis of
discussions and workshops with other Working Groups and
Steering Committee using the above analysis;
(xi)   preparing (from above) a schematic structure plan for the UB
region to include a map indicating:
* timing, investment and broad locations for upgrading/reshaping
existing Ger areas and provision of new housing development
areas, commercial areas and markets;
* location and phasing of land release and development for ger
areas and where necessary, for the expanding city, together
with key markets, and transport terminals (so as to minimize
journey to work and dangers of pollution and accidents);
* an open space strategy to link with the environmental analysis
of  suitable  areas,  and  the  population's  recreational
requirements;
(xii)   preparing key policy statements on:
* overall development policy and spatial policy;
* Ger development and role of local communities in
development planning;
- infrastructure service levels and standards;
* transport;
* social, health and educational facility standards; and
*  environmental quality and pollution control;
(xiii)   recommending a high-priority set of policy actions and public and
private investments for short-term immediate implementation and
any studies required to analyze areas of uncertainty or gather key
information required to make decisions.
(b)   Legal and Institutional Strengthening.  This Study activity would
involve:
(i)   analyzing and make recommendations regarding functions and
responsibilities of agencies and institutions operating in the urban
sector at the central and local government level and community
and private sector levels, which undertake policy formation,



-102 -                     ANNEX6
regulation, financing (investment and O&M), planning and
implementation of services and management of land;
(ii)   identifying outputs of the above agencies and their staffing
structure and assessing their strengths and adequacy for their tasks;
(iii)   identifying strengths, shortfalls, duplications, mismatches and
weak coordination between agencies;
(iv)   identifying underlying reasons for strengths and weaknesses in
regulation, planning, management of urban services by the public
sector agencies, with particular focus on poor definition of
responsibilities,  inappropriate  staff  skills,  weak  career
opportunities, low remuneration and any other disincentives
affecting action and interpersonal/interagency cooperation;
(v)    briefly assessing management and implementation capacity of the
private sector for investment in and delivery of urban services and
identifying its potential for private sector to undertake
responsibility for implementation and management of some
services; also identifying areas in this topic that may need further
study;
(vi)   from the above analysis and in the light of the projected demands
for urban services, and information/analysis of urban finance
potential, recommending legal, institutional and organizational
changes necessary to implement and manage the regulation,
planning, financing implementation and management of urban land
and services; and inasmuch as Ger areas may be planned and
developed at the micro level by their residents and Khoroo and
Duuregs, recommending any improvements and initiatives needed
by government to coordinate the outreach and assistance to Ger
residents and organize community participation (including NGO
involvement) in planning and implementation of Ger land,
services, social facilities and income generation opportunities;
(vii)   recommending measures necessary to create an institutional
structure and incentives among government agencies and the
Community to continue coordinating the planning, programming,
budgeting and monitoring of development changes. Identifying
instruments necessary to achieve this purpose (e.g., GIS mapping,
multiyear intersectoral budgeting, etc.) and assessing and
recommending the practicality of their application in Mongolia.
(c)   Financial Management Planning. This Study activity would involve the
preparation of:



-103 -                     ANNEX6
(i)   analyzing revenue by source, for central and local government
(separately for loans and grants) and including funds from external
(international) sources; estimation of amounts raised by specific
instruments (taxes, user charges, profit from government
enterprises, etc.); reviewing revenue levels over each of past five
years; and identifying any revenues tied to expenditures or specific
services or infrastructures;
(ii)   projecting revenues by source and year over the next five years,
under a do-nothing scenario, but including any recent changes in
revenue sources (e.g., sale of land leases through land privatization
program), or major Study proposals for changing or adding
specific sources of revenue;
(iii)   analyzing expenditures by year over past five years by:
* Central Government through Central implementing agencies to
specific services in UB and subsidies to urban services
corporations/companies;
*  Central Government to local government through block grants
and tied to specific services but both implemented by local
government;
* by grants and/or loans from external (international and bilateral
agencies) for services in UB.
(iv)   recommending from which sources, in what amounts and on what
potential terms and conditions (user charges, taxes, fees, bonds,
etc.) and by whom various services could be provided-e.g.,
Central Government, MUB, households, the private sector
(Developers), international agencies, banks and so on to fund the
recommended high-priority set of policy actions and investments
for short-term immediate implementation, produced under sections
above.
Study Organization
83.   The Study would be guided by a Steering Committee, whose Chairman would be
the MUB City Manager, and whose Member Secretary would be MUB's Chief of the
Department Of Strategic Policy And Planning. The rest of the Steering Committee would
be composed of senior officials from appropriate Departments of MUB and Central
Government and representatives from academic, public interest and private sector groups.
84.   The Study itself would be undertaken by three Working Groups for:



-104 -                     ANNEX6
(a)   Growth Strategy and Services Planning;
(b)   Legal and Institutional Strengthening; and
(c)   Financial Management Planning.
85.   The work on the Study of the three Working Groups is interdependent in time,
substance and analysis and each Group will have to provide timely inputs and outputs to
the other. Accordingly, their activities will have to be coordinated and integrated in time
and in regard to substance within a Study implementation Schedule. The responsibility
for coordinating working group activities, inputs and outputs would rest with the Project
Management Unit (PMU) for the UB Urban Services Project.
86.   The PMU's USIP Project Manager, or alternatively a Study Manager/Coordinator
(PMC), drawn from the private sector, nominated by the Chairman of the Steering
Committee and endorsed by the rest of the Steering Committee, would coordinate and
manage the Study within the PIU for USIP. If the Study PMC is recruited from the
private sector, his/her costs would be covered by USIP technical assistance funds.
87.   The Working Groups' members would be professional staff drawn on deputation
from relevant MUB and Central Government Ministries and Departments and from
Community, and private sector groups with interests in the Study.
88.   All Working Group MUB and Central Government members would be deputized
full-time to the Working Groups and would be paid by Government through normal
channels. Community and private sector representatives would participate at their own
cost.
89.   The PMU and Study Working Groups would be assisted in the management,
analysis and organization of the Study by International Consultants, from whom MUB
seeks proposals under these Terms of Reference. The PMU and Study Working Groups
would also be assisted in the management, analysis and organization of the Study by
Local Consultants wno would be separately funded by USIP. The PMU would identify
the Local Consultants and the Study PMC if recruited from the private sector. The
appointment by the PMC of Local Consultants and the PMC to undertake the Study,
would be subject to review and approval by: (a) IDA; and (b) the International
Consultants.
90.   The Consultants will be expected to provide technical assistance to MUB/PMU
and its Study Working Groups over a period of six months commencing, if possible, in
early 1998. During the opening period of the Study, substantial advice/guidance is likely
to be needed, particuiarly regarding study methodologies and their implementation.
Subsequently, once procedures, working methods, the Working Groups and the Local
Consultants have been established the need for technical advice and the intensity of
International Consultants' assistance may reduce somewhat in the middle stage of the
Study.



-105 -                     ANNEX6
91.   The Consultant will provide about 7 person-months of professional staff input
over the six-month Study period, and will appoint a Study Leader as their prime
representative (table annexed). The Team Leader will be a senior urban planner, with
substantial experience in planning and management of urban services and finance, and-
if possible-having appropriate regional experience.

-110 -                     ANNEX8
ANNEX 8: SCHEDULE OF PROCUREMENT PACKAGES
1.    Project preparation has reached detailed design for the physical components of
water supply and ger upgrading. The Project Preparation Unit issued a table listing all
proposed packages for each year, which was discussed during the appraisal mission and
agreed. It was also agreed as part of the Sector Action Plan that the Project Management
Unit (PMU) would submit an updated procurement plan each October for every year after
Year 1 implementation. The revised table is based on the following constraints: the
limited period of the six-month growing season followed by harsh winter and freezing
conditions that hamper civil works to be carried out over winter; and the implementation
capacity of the local construction industry.  Both factors are good incentives for
developing bidding plans along yearly schedules with the requirements that bids need to
be distributed in the month of December preceding the year of implementation, and
approved at the latest the next March to enable contractors to proceed as soon as frost
conditions allow  excavation and construction activities.  Bidding documentation,
definition of packages and type of procurement have been comprehensively discussed
with the Project Unit and Municipality officials for the first year of implementation. All
procurement of equipment for amount over $100,000 would be carried out by ICB. All
procurement for civil works above $500,000 would be carried out according to ICB using
sample bid documents. Civil works for lesser amounts would be carried out on an NCB
basis using bidding documentation developed on the basis of the Sample Bid Document
for smaller works and prepared in the Mongolian language. All bid documents for the
first year of implementation will receive a-priori review, to ensure their adequacy of their
technical, commercial and general specifications. Packages and modes of procurement
for subsequent yearly work programs would proceed on the same basis as the schedule of
the first year of implementation.
2.    The metering and fixture repair programs would have the equipment packaged in
three parts and procured by the PMU on behalf of USAG during the first three years.
Under the metering package, the supplier would be asked to provide technical assistance
to help the kantors implement installation of meters according to best practices; and to
provide the software billing and collection systems.  Installation of meters and
replacement of fixtures would also be would be carried out by the kantors. Likewise, the
repair of fixtures would also be carried out by the kantors' labor force.
3.    Year-I and 2 Bid Packages. The table below gives a tentative breakdown of the
various goods and works into bid packages for the first two years and a tentative
breakdown of the program for subsequent years.

such matters as procurement, disbursement is estimated at 3 weeks annually from FY98
through FY02. Accordingly, the total IDA supervision input is estimated at 75 staff-
weeks, of which 20 staff-weeks would be at the headquarters, 40 staff-weeks in the field,
and 15 staff-weeks would be from the Resident Mission's staff.

Borrower's Contribution to Supervision
2.    Overall coordination of the project would be the responsibility of the Project
Management Unit. This would include, inter alia, (a) providing guidance on timing and
form of reporting implementation progress; (b) arranging meetings with the government,
water company during mission's field visits; (c) ensuring timely information to IDA; and
(d) proposing satisfactory resolution of any unexpected issue occurring during
implementation.
3.    The following reports on implementation progress would be submitted to IDA:
semiannually:  (a) reports on implementation status, including monitoring of the
Environmental Management Plan (EMP); annually: (b) annual updated financial
projections of water company; (c) audited financial statements of water company and
Special Account for the credit including a separate opinion on Statement of Expenditures;
and other: (d) evaluation report on project implementation and any report required on
various subjects.
Outline of Semiannual Status Report
4.    The semiannual status report should comprise a descriptive part and appendices;
the descriptive part should not exceed 20 pages and should cover the following items:
(a)   General Description of the Project. A concise summary of project
objectives, description cities' components, financing, initial and latest
estimated costs, initial and latest schedule, key data of credit and project
agreements and of various amendments;
(b)   Project Overview, Physical Accomplishment. Status of implementation
progress for each component of the project, procurement, information on
inspection of manufacturing, input of supervising consultant; monitoring
of EMP compliance during the reported period;
(c)   Procurement Status. List of awarded contracts with dates of award, start
and completion of contract, allowed time extensions, and variations,
contract amounts in various currencies and status of disbursement for
contracts, projected and actual disbursement S-curve for the total cost;
(d)   Institutional Status. Progress of institutional improvements in water
company, performance of advisers and achievements in staff training;
(e)   Problems Affecting Implementation.  Description of problems and
proposed or taken actions.
5.    Appendices will list personnel provided by the provinces and water company, by
the consultants and by contractors, key meetings, important visits on site, photographs,



-119-                       ANNEX 11
location plans of the project showing planned and achieved progress and monitoring
indicators. The report will include key monitoring indicators shown in Annex 3.
Outline of the Evaluation Report on Project Implementation
6.    The evaluation report to be prepared by USAG and PMU, should not exceed 10
pages and the performance judgments and analyses should cover the following specific
aspects:
(a)   Project Objectives.  A concise statement and evaluation of major
objectives as presented in the SAR, including any changes made during
implementation. The evaluation will assess the clarity of the objectives,
their realism, and whether they are important for the country and sector.
(b)   Achievement of Project Objectives. An assessment of the project's
success (substantial, partial, negligible) in achieving its major objectives,
e.g., sector policy improvements, financial objectives, institutional
development, physical objectives, other social objectives, environmental
objectives, and public sector management and private sector development.
(c)   Implementation Record and Major Factors Affecting the Project. An
analysis of the factors that affect implementation. The factors are divided
into those not generally subject to government or provinces control, those
subject to government and provinces control, and those subject to water
company control.
(d)   IDA  Performance.  An assessment of IDA's performance (highly
satisfactory, satisfactory, deficient) in project identification, preparation
assistance, appraisal, and supervision.
(e)   Water Company Performance. An assessment of the performance of
involved water company and municipality (highly satisfactory,
satisfactory, deficient) in preparing, implementing, and operating the
project.
(f)    Assessment of Outcome.  A rating of outcome (highly satisfactory,
satisfactory, unsatisfactory, or highly unsatisfactory) based primarily on
how well the project achieved its objectives and how sustainable it is
likely to be.
(g)   Future Operation. A description and assessment of the plan for the
project's future operation, including understanding the measures to
maximize the project benefits, and the indicators for monitoring and
evaluating future operations.



-120-                      ANNEX 11
(h)   Key Lessons Learned. A discussion of the most significant positive and
negative lessons learned from the project's implementation, showing how
they are reflected in the arrangements for its future operations. This
section also identifies those lessons most relevant to similar ongoing and
future projects in this sector in Mongolia.
7.    MUB and USAG will submit the evaluation report to IDA after completing the
project and in time stipulated in the Project Agreement.



-121-                      ANNEX 12
ANNEX 12: FINANCIAL ANALYSIS OF ULAANBAATAR
MUNICIPALITY
OVERVIEW
1.    Ulaanbaatar, with more than 25 percent of Mongolia's total population, had an
operating budget of Tug 23.9 billion in 1995, equivalent to 18 percent of the national
budget of Tug 131.3 billion. Due to the economic upheaval of the early 1990s, the
budget of the Municipality of Ulaanbaatar (MUB) shrank dramatically in 1992 and then
stabilized in real terms ever since then as did its size relative to the national budget. The
MUB budget is divided into the current budget for operating expenditures and the capital
budget for new investments. MUB has no outstanding debts and its revenues have grown
in line with inflation since 1992. The expected introduction of a property tax on the
recently privatized apartments and MUB's policy of increasing tariffs for urban services
to recover operating costs will help further consolidate its finances.
2.    The proposed IDA credit will be directly onlent from the central government to
USAG. MUB, however, agreed to provide $1.26 million as counterpart funding and had
already earmarked Tug 200 million in the 1997 budget as part of the project's counterpart
funding to carry out the construction of two connecting pipelines to support the project.
The project will not create any excessive burden on MUB as IDA's credit will be paid
back by the expected revenues of USAG.
3.    The AusAID grant of $5 million will be passed on to the municipality. The
investments supported by AusAID for the next five years amounts to 50 percent of
MUB's 1996 capital budget and will substantially help relieve the burden of municipal
finance. The AusAID component will relieve some of MUB's financial burden for ger
investments by introducing the concept of cost recovery and establishing a Ger
Infrastructure Development Fund.
PAST PERFORMANCE
4.    The operating budget accounts for around 75 percent of the total budget. Taxes
are the major source of operating budget, accounting for around 80 percent. The business
income tax is the largest source but with declining shares at over 40 percent currently,
followed by the trade tax and personal income tax with 14 and 12 percent, respectively.
On the expenditure side, the personnel cost, including a social security contribution, is the
largest cost, with its share increasing from 30 percent in 1994 to 48 percent in 1996. By
sector, education and health accounts for 80 percent of total expenditure. Except in 1995,
the operating budget has recorded small annual surpluses, which were used as a source
for the capital budget in the following year.



-122 -                     ANNEX 12
5.    The capital budget as a proportion of the operating budget was as high as 50
percent in 1991 when subsidies from the central government were large. Since then, its
size relative to the operating budget has been stable at around 25 percent. Its source
includes the carryover surplus from the current budget, and revenues from special funds
such as the housing sales fund, road fund, and fixed asset sales and leases. An additional
source is the subsidy from the central government. One major source of the central
government subsidy, the apartment construction fund provided by Russian aid, dropped
dramatically in 1992 and was discontinued from 1994.
6.   In constant terms, both the current and capital budgets shrank substantially in
1992, mainly due to an exchange rate adjustment and hyperinflation that began in 1991.
The consequent reduction was more dramatic in the capital budget, which dropped to 14
percent in 1992 from the previous year in constant terms. As MUB did not adjust its
urban services fees to catch up with inflation, it scaled down its operation and
maintenance as well as new investments. This also brought about gradual deterioration of
the quality of urban services. Even with these adjustments, however, many urban
operations such as transportation, solid waste, and housing services resulted in losses,
which were subsequently covered by subsidies from the central government. Realizing
that this practice is not sustainable and results in waste and inefficiencies, the government
allowed tariff increases to cover the operating cost of services from 1994.
FUTURE DEVELOPMENT
7.    Currently, MUB does not prepare a detailed medium-term budget projection. It
hopes that it can meet the increasing demand for better urban services by various
initiatives. It will continue to push for tariff increases for various urban services to
recover the cost of services. It is also endeavoring to increase efficiency of service
delivery by introducing competition and privatization where warranted.
8.    One of the most important initiatives is the imminent housing privatization, which
will also affect the municipal budget. The privatization of apartments will allow the
Municipality to charge apartment residents fees that better reflect the cost of services.
More importantly, however, this will provide a basis for charging the new owners with
property taxes. The total value of apartments in MUB, with space totaling 22 million m2,
is estimated at around Tug 330 billion. Although the timing, the tax rate as well as the
allocation of tax revenue between the central and municipal government need to be
decided in the future, the introduction of a property tax on privatized apartments will give
the municipal budget a major boost.
9.    MUB also recently launched the MUB Infrastructure Development Fund, initially
funded by donations, and plans to expand the fund with various sources including
auctioning off land tenures for buildings, charging immigration fees into MUB and other
miscellaneous fees. To be more effective, this fund will require more sustainable funding
sources.



-123 -                     ANNEX 12
10.   Despite the various initiatives already introduced, MUB realizes that it needs a
more comprehensive and systematic approach to improve its municipal finance to meet
the increasing and changing need for urban services and infrastructure in a transitional
economy. It plans to carry out a study on municipal finance with the support of a
bilateral donor.
11.   MUB's cash flow projection, which includes the proposed project investment and
debt services, is developed and attached. Even with conservative assumption of no
property tax from apartments, it confirms the fact that MUB will not face cash flow
problems as the cash inflow from USAG more than offsets its cash outflow to GOM.
Moreover, actual revenues of MUB are expected to be greater, with the introduction of
property tax on apartments.

-127 -                       ANNEX 13
ANNEX 13: FINANCIAL ANALYSIS, PROJECTIONS AND
ASSUMPTIONS FOR USAG
A. BASIC ASSUMPTIONS
1.    Price Escalation. In line with the Bank's forecasts, domestic and foreign price
escalation was estimated as follows.
Year                  1997  1998  1999  2000  2001  2002  2003  2004  2005
Local Inflation(%)     25.5  20.0   20    10    10    10    10    10    10
Intemational Inflation(%)    2.5   2.5   2.5   2.5   2.5   2.5   2.5   2.5   2.5
Exchange Rate (Tug/$)  753   926  1,088  1,279  1,395  1,478  1,588  1,708  1,836
2.    Tariff Rate. The new tariff structure effective from October 1996 is assumed to
be committed to apply during 1997 for the analysis. The tariffs, however, are assumed to
be escalated in line with inflation from 1998 on. For the "with project" case, however,
three major changes are assumed:
(a)    Tariffs charged to ger area residents would fall to the same level as those
for apartment dwellers owing to the introduction of piped water supply in
ger areas;
(b)   A one-step tariff system will be introduced for apartment dwellers with the
installation of block meters. In the base case, consumption over 230 lcd
will be charged at the same rate as for industrial consumers. Accordingly,
average consumption for apartment residents is assumed to reduce to
230 lcd for the initial years. In practice, the criteria as well as the penalty
rate will be adjusted based on feedback from consumption patterns;
(c)    To ease the transition to a metering system from the current norm of
150 lcd, the tariff for apartment dwellers is assumed to decrease to a level
so that there will be no increase in the water bill if they consume 230 lcd.
3.    Block Metering. Installation of block meters will be completed during the first
three years of the project for all apartment units and industries. Installation of individual
meters will be encouraged, but would remain optional.
B. INCOME STATEMENT ASSUMPTIONS
4.    Capital Cost. The project's capital cost was developed on the basis of a detailed
schedule of capital equipment and civil works requirements. In addition, $2 million



-128 -                     ANNEX 13
investment in 1997 is assumed to be supplied by grant from JICA to help install meters in
CTPs and to fund other urgent repairs.
5.    Onlending Conditions. IDA funds will be onlent from the central govermnent to
USAG with the following terms and conditions:
Maturity:      20 years with a grace period of 5 years
Interest Rate  5 percent
6.    Electricity Price. The new electricity price which was increased over 60 percent
in 1996 is used as a base cut, which is then assumed to increase with the general inflation
rate.
7.    Special Fund for Maintenance and Repair. To address the problem of curtailed
budget for repair and maintenance, a special fund for maintenance and repair will be set
up. The current expenditure for maintenance will be increased by 10 percent in real terms
for the next five years and will remain at that level, with adjustments for inflation.
8.    Depreciation.  As most of USAG's existing assets were revalued recently,
historical depreciation will continue to be used for the existing assets.  For new
equipment, 25 percent declining balance depreciation will be used while civil works will
be depreciated with a 40-year straight-line method.
9.    Water Production  Currently, uncharged water amounts to 49 percent of
USAG's total water production. This is a combination of Unaccounted For Water (UFW)
and uncharged consumption of nonmetered consumers. It is assumed that uncharged
consumption of nonmetered consumers will drastically decline so that UFW will be 25
percent.
10.   Collection Ratio. In view of the transition to a new tariff structure, and metering
and possibly collection method, conservative assumptions are adopted for the collection
ratio despite the improvement in the kantor charging system. The collection ration of
institutions and apartments will increase gradually to a normal level.

-132 -                     ANNEX 14
ANNEX 14: ECONOMIC ANALYSIS OF THE PROJECT
CHOICE OF SECTOR
1.    The quality of urban services in Mongolia has deteriorated badly since the
withdrawal of Soviet assistance in 1990 and the resulting economic crisis. Since 1990,
most capital investment and expansion projects have been halted, and rehabilitation, even
minor repairs of existing assets, has been severely curtailed. In general, the formal utility
services face severe equipment problems. All equipment originated from FSU countries
and is now difficult to replace or maintain with spares because of the severance of
relationships with previous suppliers and inadequate hard currency to pay for imports.
For instance, more than 10 percent of the wells in the well fields supplying Ulaanbaatar
are inoperative due to faulty pumps; the municipal sewage plant treats only 75 percent of
the sewage due to defective pumps and equipment; and pipe breaks take three to four
days to repair due to lack of materials and equipment.
2.    Low levels of cost recovery, typical of the Soviet era when users contributed little
and utilities received substantial subsidies for new investments, have exacerbated service
delivery problems. Metering-whether water, heat, or power-on apartment blocks or
individual premises is almost nonexistent. Water and sewerage are billed on the basis of
consumption norms, and collection of bills. While tariff levels cover full costs of the
present low level of operations and depreciation, they would be insufficient to cover costs
required to fund adequate levels of maintenance and rehabilitation, much less new
investments in the systems.
3.    In mid-1994, the Government's concern about the condition of Mongolia's cities,
which had begun to show the effects of several years of forced neglect, caused it to
request Association assistance in carrying out an informal review of urban services in
Mongolia's main cities. In particular, urban issues were assessed from the perspective of
two government goals:   poverty alleviation and devolution of functions and
responsibilities to local governments. The findings of the review were discussed at
length with the Government, and the proposed project designed to meet the twin goals of
poverty alleviation and improved sector efficiency. In preparing project assistance to the
poor ger areas, a menu of possible improvements was discussed with representatives of
the 12 ger communities, who were able to choose the specific types of investments most
needed in their areas.  Consultation with the representatives continued throughout
preparation. The result is a project tailored to the needs of both the Municipality and the
ger residents and strong ownership of the initiatives planned, and balanced with
Mongolia's debt servicing capacity.



-133 -                      ANNEX 14
4.    Ger Infrastructure Improvements-Economic Evaluation.  Planned Ger
improvement covers various subcomponents reflecting the priorities of the Ger residents,
generating various economic benefits. Most of these benefits, though hard to quantify,
are substantial and directly accrue to Ger residents. Provision of total length of 10.8 km
roads, a bridge, and 28.1 km all-weather footpaths with four footbridges in areas with the
most critical needs can help save substantial time for the residents to haul coal and wood,
other household goods, as well as to fetch water. For example, the maximum travel
distance for 20 kg water fetching will be reduced from the maximum of 700 m to 350 m,
and the footpaths will help ease the labor of water fetching usually carried out by women
and children. The benefit from easier fuel hauling is no less significant, considering the
bulky nature of coal and wood and its position as the second largest expenditure item in
Ger areas. The estimated time saved or water fetching alone would amount to over 1O
million hours/year for 86,000 people, and similar saving is expected for coal, wood and
other household supplies. Paved roads can also substantially save the fuel cost and
maintenance expenditure for delivery trucks and other vehicles, as well as save the time
for delivery.  The economic rate of return for $1.4 million investment for road
construction is estimated to be over 40 percent. Construction and operation of public
bathhouses and laundries can improve the health of Ger residents.  Likewise,
improvement in solid waste collection would improve the hygiene of Ger areas
considerably. Equally important are the benefits from the construction of the stormwater
drainage in flood-prone areas, which can help reduce the property damage as well as
avoid the loss of human lives. In addition to these direct benefits, overall infrastructure
improvements would motivate the voluntary investments for further improvements in
each individual plot by each household. Some of these benefits would be captured by the
increase in property prices, particularly in light of the forthcoming privatization of urban
land. Poor families, in particular, are likely to aim to increase their income by renting
space to other families. The large plots and low density of development in Ulaanbaatar
ger area provide substantial scope for rental units. Because of the lack of a legal property
market in UB, the rapidly-shifting effects of the transition from public to private
ownership and from a managed to a market economy and the lack of data on land prices,
the above-mentioned benefits from upgrading have not been quantified. The benefits
captured in land prices change over time, also depend closely on the location of particular
Ger areas in relation to city economic activity centers, transportation services and city
economic growth. However, the experience with slum upgrading for low-income
populations in other countries is that unit land prices experience a one-time increase of
between 25 and 27 percent of preimprovement value, while the expenditure on
investment in housing and related on-plot infrastructure, such as sanitation, jumps from a
few percent of household income to as much as 30 percent or more of household income.
OVERVIEW
5.    The project comprises three major components: water supply in the Ger area,
water supply in central city area, and Ger area upgrading. As each component's
economic benefit has different features, the analysis is carried out by component.



-134-                       ANNEX 14
WATER SUPPLY INVESTMENTS
6.    In the Ger area, the benefits and costs are quantified to the extent possible. The
major benefit is the increased consumption of water to the level of 20 lcd, which is
considered the minimum requirement for health and hygiene. This benefit is measured
using the 1995 price of water adjusted to the end-1996 price level as a proxy for
consumers' willingness to pay. Additional benefits are derived from the reduction of
capital investments to purchase tanks and trucks and recurrent costs to maintain a larger
fleet of trucks as a result of piped water supply to kiosks to cover 70 percent of the Ger
area population. The EIRR is estimated at 18 percent. It should be noted that the major
benefits of the project, measured as a gap between willingness to pay and actual payment,
will accrue to Ger residents. There are other substantial benefits, although difficult to
quantify. The additional consumption of potable water will substantially enhance the
level of hygiene in Ger areas, and project investments to facilitate access to the public
water kiosks will also help relieve the labor burden of women and children, who are
traditionally responsible for hauling water.
7.    Benefit-cost analysis for water supply investments in the central city area
compares the incremental costs and benefits of the "with project" and "without project"
cases, using shadow prices to reflect economic costs to the country. Unlike traditional
projects, the benefits come from the reduced consumption of valuable resources rather
than from incremental benefits or production. In fact, the water production will decrease
with the project. The "with project" case assumes that, with the introduction of block
metering and step tariffs, the wastage of water will substantially decrease, requiring fewer
resources capital investments to purchase tanks and trucks. The benefits of treating less
wastewater as well as using fewer resources for water production are captured as USAG
covers both operations. The resultant EIRR of 14 percent, however, underrepresented the
benefits since the unquantifiable benefits of having a more stable water supply with
higher water pressure backed up by reservoirs have been excluded from the analysis.
Another major nonquantifiable benefit is the enhanced efficiency and accountability
derived from institutional strengthening supported by project technical assistance.
8.    The weighted average EIRR for the water supply components is estimated to be
15 percent, although this excludes the significant unquantifiable benefits of better
maintenance and repair, which would reduce future capital investment requirements
considerably, benefits of easier access to the piped water in Ger areas, and benefits from
the institutional strengthening component such as improved management and
strengthened accountability.
GER AREA INFRASTRUCTURE IMPROVEMENTS
9.    Ger Infrastructure Improvements-Economic Evaluation.  Ger upgrading
would increase property values and property rentals, particularly in light of the
forthcoming privatization of urban land, which will almost certainly strengthen ger tenure
rights. Experience in other countries also shows that the upgrading, combined with better



-135-                      ANNEX14
tenure tights encourages a majority of households to increase the investment on their
property in housing, on-plot sanitation and rental units. Additional indirect benefits of
upgrading coupled with tenure improvements are increases in property tax revenues-
which are likely to be imposed in Ulaanbaatar with the privatization of land and increases
in household income. Poor families, in particular, are likely to aim to increase their
income by renting space to other families.  The large plots and low density of
development in Ulaanbaatar ger area provide substantial scope for rental units. Because
of the lack of a legal property market in UB, the rapidly-shifting effects of the transition
from public to private ownership and from a managed to a market economy and the lack
of data on land prices, the above-mentioned benefits from upgrading have not been
quantified. The benefits captured in land prices change over time, also depend closely on
the location of particular Ger areas in relation to city economic activity centers,
transportation services and city economic growth. However, the experience with slum
upgrading for low-income populations in other countries is that unit land prices
experience a one-time increase of between 25 and 27 percent of preimprovement value,
while the expenditure on investment in housing and related on-plot infrastructure, such as
sanitation, jumps from a few perfect of household income to as much as 30 percent or
more of household income. Upgrading would result in large additional unquantified
benefits including: savings in the time and energy expended mostly by women and
children who fetch and haul water from kiosks to homes; improvements and health and
savings in time lost from school and work due to illness; and avoided costs of damage to
property due to flooding.
ASSUMPTIONS FOR ECONOMIC ANALYSIS OF GER AREA WATER SUPPLY
10.   All benefits and costs are expressed in constant 1996 monetary terms. Price
escalation as well as taxes and duties are excluded. The economic internal rate of return
(EIRR) is calculated for the 20-year period with a discount rate of 12 percent.
Revenue
11.   Water Consumption. Without the project, actual per capita water consumption
was estimated at 9.5 lcd in the Ger area. The project will gradually increase the per capita
water consumption to 20 lcd, which is regarded as the minimum hygienic requirement
and is consistent with the typical average amount consumed by low-income households
who must carry their water from standpipes. In addition, total water consumption is
expected to increase in line with the population increase estimated to be 2.2 percent per
year.
12.   Willingness to Pay. As a proxy for the economic benefit, the unit water price of
Tug 400 in 1995 was used. This was adjusted to the constant 1996 price at Tug 600, and
the new tariff rate was deducted to measure the net benefit.



-136 -                       ANNEX 14
Capital Cost
13.   Investment. Investment costs including physical contingencies for the Ger area
piped water supply system are used for the "with project" case.
14.   Tanker Trucks. USAG' tanker subsidiary currently has 49 tanker trucks. Under
the "without project" case, the required tanks and trucks is assumed to gradually increase
to 50 each for the 10-year period, while under the "with project" case, the same number
would decrease to 30, reflecting the growing increase of the piped supply to kiosks. The
average price for a new truck and tank is assumed to be $18,000 and $900, respectively,
and the average life of a tank and a truck is assumed to be 10 years each.
Recurrent Costs
15.   Recurrent operating and maintenance costs are divided into three components:
salary and related expenses, fuel and vehicle related expenses, and others. Fuel and other
vehicle-related costs are directly affected by the number of trucks and tanks. Salary-
related expenses and other costs are also adjusted accordingly. The reduction in salaries
and "others" are transferred to the central city area benefit-cost analysis, as the extra
human resources are assumed to be absorbed in the central city area operation.
Economic Cost
16.   Conversion Factor. The conversion factors used to convert electricity price is
1.33 for the first three years, and this will gradually converge to 1.0 in 2002 in line with
the expected price increase. As for the labor cost, a conversion factor of 1.4 was used to
reflect the lagging wage adjustments under high inflation.
17.   Sensitivity Test. A test to measure the sensitivity of EIRR of the water supply
component was carried out for major variables, including different level of water
consumption by ger residents as well as apartment residents. If the average water
consumption by ger residents turns out to be 15 lcd and 25 lcd, rather than assumed
20 lcd, the resultant EIRR would change to 14 and 17.8 percent, respectively. Likewise,
if the average apartment resident consume 200 lcd and 250 lcd of water instead of 230 lcd
in the base case, the EIRR would be 16.7 and 14.4 percent, respectively. This is
consistent with the analysis, as the major sources of economic benefits are increased
welfare in Ger area and the savings of valuable resources from the reduction of waste in
apartments. Thus, even under the unfavorable scenarios of lower water consumption in
Ger area or less-than-expected level of wastage reduction in apartments, the project
remains viable. It is also important to note that, under both scenarios, the effect on the
financial viability of the project is negligible.

ANNEX 15: GER AREA UPGRADING AND COST RECOVERY
BACKGROUND
1.    Population. The total Ger population is estimated to be about 300,000 in 1996.
Since 1991, it had been decreasing slightly, but recently started to slowly increase as
apartment construction practically ceased.
2.    About 45 percent of UB's population live in the Ger areas. The density of Ger
areas is very low, varying from 54 persons/ha in Nisek to 198 persons/ha in Gandan in
the flat, low-lying city center. A significant number of people keep animals on their plots
and some have gardens. Except for deeply-ravined Shar Khad, the population in the flat
areas to the east of the city in Bayanzurkh had been decreasing, but is likely to increase in
the future.
3.    The population forecasts suggest that the population of the Ger Areas could
double by about 2012, if gers grow faster than the rest of the city. There appear to be few
options to accommodate this increase in suitable (existing) areas, where infrastructure can
be provided.
4.    The three areas that topographically are most suited to accommodate the growth
are the flat areas in Bayanzurkh, mentioned above, the Naddam celebration site near the
airport to the southeast of the city and the central wellfield area, if other water sources can
be identified. Each of these options poses planning and development difficulties and in
order to assess these and any other options, further study is required.
5.    Incomes. Ger household incomes (in 1995/96) were as low as Tug 15,000 ($25
equivalent), or a median income of about Tug 30,000 in 1995 and Tug 40,000 in 1996,
respectively (or $55 and $60, respectively). A sample survey in 1996 indicated that
average ger incomes were about Tug 60,000, if capital expenditures were included.
Many of the Ger residents appear to be elderly pensioners, the unemployed or
underemployed and single-parent female households, for whom in the absence of
subsidized apartment housing, the Ger is an affordable housing solution.  Solid
information about apartment incomes is unavailable but it is probably safe to say that Ger
incomes are about 70 percent of those of people in apartments.
6.    Ger Locations. Most of the Gers-except for Yarmag and Nisek-are located on
the north bank of the Tuul River, the city's public water source. With the exception of
the Gandan area, the majority of Ger area is in the periphery of the city at a distance of 2
to 12 km from the built-up area of UB, which parallels the Tuul River (Table 1 below).
Many of the Ger population live on the hills to the north of the city along the Selbe and
Tolgoit River valleys, where the Ger population had been mostly increasing. These areas



-141-                      ANNEX 15
are drained by natural ravines, which increasingly erode their banks, due to dumping of
solid waste in them by Ger residents. The disruption of drainage channels combined with
a lack of paved, stabilized or graded roads, limits access by water trucks, buses and other
vehicles and also hampers residents' access to and transport from water kiosks, especially
in winter.
TABLE 1: GER AREAS

(Level and Drained)      (Deeply Ravined)     (Flood Plain Developments)
