/a Areas to receive project infrastructure improvements.
7.    During the winter months, there is severe low-level atmospheric pollution of the
City arising from the burning of coal and wood in the basic stoves used in all gers. There
have been many studies into ways of alleviating this situation, including the raising of the
height of the flues. A possible project to import Liquefied Petroleum Gas (LPG) for
domestic use and by industry exists. The estimated annual cost for ger fuel consumption
is among the largest items of household expenditure.
8.    An increasing number of the Ger population live at altitudes above that of the
existing reservoirs close to the city and the new reservoirs to be constructed under the
project. This form of settlement needs to be constrained by a combination of revisions in
land use in the Master Plan (para. 10 below), land regulation and institutions, and
arrangements to provide or withhold services (such as water supply).
9.    Planning Considerations. Until recently, Ger areas were considered to be
temporary until apartment accommodation was built. Occupants were only given a right
to occupy by the Municipal Duuregs for an unspecified time and sometimes after
occupation of sites had taken place. This weak, impermanent form of tenure, combined
with generally low Ger incomes, inhibits investment in house construction.



-142 -                     ANNEX 15
10.   The current Master Plan was prepared in 1986, before the massive changes in
Mongolia's political and economic situation forced the curtailment of the apartment
building program. In the longer run, the inefficient current Ger layout in long lanes and
large (400-700 m 2) plots, without reservations for community open space and facilities,
will need to be redeveloped. The Master Plan needs to be revised to incorporate the
current situation in which the majority of the Ger areas are likely to develop into
permanent residential areas. Also, the form of tenure to be provided to Ger residents
under the ongoing (in 1997) program to privatize housing and land should take into
account, if possible, future needs to restructure the infrastructure and reassemble plots in
a more efficient configuration, suitable for an urban environment.
11.   Water Supply. Since the 1960s, truck tanker water supply replaced wells or
horse-borne transport; and in the 1970s water-dispensing kiosks were introduced. But the
service is unreliable.
12.   Water is usually fetched from kiosks to homes, in small-wheeled handcarts,
loaded with up to 40 kg of water, over a distance of 200-700 m, mostly by women and
children, over rough hilly, unstable and unsurfaced earth paths and roads. In the winter,
ice and snow impede access to the kiosks by both tanker trucks and ger residents and
water supply is erratic. Ger consumption from kiosks is about 8 lcd; the amount is
limited by both cost and the difficulty of transporting the water and is well below the
minimum requirement for good health and sanitation of 20 lcd. The total water
consumed in Ger areas, although probably underestimated by USAG, is only 2.2 million
m3/day, not even 2 percent of USAG's daily demand.
13.   The high costs of tankered water have been inequitably passed through to Ger
residents; they pay 20-30 times more per unit of water than apartment residents, whose
costs are subsidized by institutional water consumers (industrial, commercial and public
premises). Because of the high price of water and the difficulties in transporting it from
kiosks to homes, many Ger residents supplement their water supply from other,
sometimes unsanitary sources: rivers, surface water, wells and rainwater. During project
preparation, the Municipality of Ulaanbaatar (MUB) recognized the inequity of Ger and
apartment water charges and held ger unit charges constant while increasing apartment
charges by over 50 percent.
14.   The principal thrust of the project is to replace high-cost tankered water with
much lower-priced and better-quality water, more consistently available, from the piped
system. Standards and designs for the project water supply component are discussed in
Annexes 1. 2 and 4.
15.   Health and Sanitation. The inequity in and inadequacy of public services (lack
of adequate heating, water, sanitation and social facilities) compounded by low incomes
leads to significant nutritional, as well as morbidity and health differences, between Ger
and apartment areas. A UNICEF nutrition survey of 1993 found that infants born into ger
area families had a 60 percent higher risk of low weight than those born in apartments.



-143 -                       ANNEX 15
Also, at least 80 percent of children in the Ger who are under five years old suffer from
diarrhea in summer and acute respiratory infections in winter.  (The equivalent
percentages of children in apartments are less than 30 and 40 percent, respectively.)
Upgrading of public services and living conditions in Ger area under the project,
particularly water supply, would help relieve the hardships of the urban poor while
improving environmental conditions with attendant health benefits.
16.   There were bathhouses and washing facilities in the Ger. However, these were
privatized in the first half of the 1990s; the prices set by MUB proved to be uneconomic
and by 1996 only a few were still operating. Many Ger residents are said, perforce, to
bathe in their relatives' apartments. Surveys indicate that lack of bathhouses is regarded
as a major problem by Ger residents.
17.   The project would provide the following bathhouse and laundry facilities in
project ger areas:
(a)    Hailast. Improvement of existing facilities and addition of laundries;
(b)   Denjin and Chingeltei. A new bathhouse including laundry facilities;
(c)    Dambadarjaa. A new bathhouse including laundry facilities, and;
(d)   Gandan. Installation of a laundry facility
18.   Most Ger plots have simple pit latrines for human waste disposal. Due to the low,
but increasing densities in Ger and the nature of soils, the existing sanitation methods,
though crude, are not a major problem, except possibly in the low-lying Ger area near the
floodplain of the Tuul River.
19.   Roads and Paths. In the Ger area roads and paths are almost entirely rough earth
unconsolidated, poorly bridged and undrained. In the deeply ravined Ger area, access for
vehicles of all types and pedestrians is difficult. The lack of storm drainage channels, the
alignment of roads, often parallel to slopes, and lack of maintenance all impede and
increase the costs of access for buses, trucks and other vehicles. In addition, the terraces
of plots and access paths to them can be extremely long (up to 600 m of continuous
frontage).
20.   Criteria for Roads and Paths. Under the project, pedestrian access proposals
are based on the following criteria:
(a)   No journey of more than 150 m should be necessary on an unmade surface
when collecting water.
(b)   The total journey to collect water should not be more than 350 m.
(c)    These distances should be actual and not "as-the-crow-flies."



-144 -                      ANNEX 15
21.   The project would provide the following roads and footpaths in project ger areas:
Gandan             Roads: 2.2 km;     Footpaths: 1.8 km
Denjin 1000        Roads: 2.8 km;     Footpaths: 10.1 km
Hailast            Roads: 0.5 km      Footpaths: 6.3 km
Chingeltei         Roads: 2.0 km      Footpaths: 5.0 km    1 Road bridge
and 3 footbridges
Dambadarjaa        Roads: 3.3 km      Footpaths: 4.9 km    1 Footbridge
22.   Design Criteria - Roads and Paths
(a)   Road Widths. Pavement: 5.5 m. Shoulders: 1.25 m. (No land acquisition
required, since existing rights-of-way are generous.
(b)   Road Pavement. Asphaltic concrete and gravel. Subbase material, CBR
35; base material, CBR 60; commercial vehicles design volume: Black
Market Road-250/day. Other roads-80/day. On this basis, all roads
except for the Black Market Road would have a total pavement thickness
of 275 mm, of which Base, 175 mm, and AC surface course, 100 mm.
The more heavily trafficked Black Market Road would be as follows: a
total pavement thickness of 400 mm, of which base, 100 mm, subbase,
200 mm and AC surface course, 100 mm.
(c)   Road Crossing of Creeks and Gullies. Reinforced concrete pipe culverts
(except Chingeltei where bridge will be standard design for a 9 m span);
Maximum velocity: 3.5 m/sec.
(d)   Footpaths. 1 m wide for access to house lanes. 1.2 m wide for collector
streets in ger areas. Alignment would be generally 0.5 m from plot fences
on the side of the street/lane opposite utilities (power, telephone poles and
proposed water lines). Footpaths would be reinforced sufficiently to
withstand a 4-ton wheel load to accommodate trucks driving with one
wheel on the footpath.
(e)   Footpaths would be located according to the following criteria:
(i)   to provide direct access to kiosks.
(ii)   no plot more than 100 m from a footpath
(iii)   highest priority to steep-slope and erosion areas.



-145 -                      ANNEX 15
Solid Waste
23.   Ger used to get door-to-door solid waste collection services. The system has
deteriorated due to lack of funds for operations and is now irregular from identifiable but
uncontrolled points that are a maximum of 500 m from houses. Consequently, residents
commonly dump their waste in drainage channels and natural ravines. Drainage capacity,
thus, has been substantially reduced, causing local flooding and property damage
sometimes. The project would provide several compactor collection trucks and about 600
3-mr3 steel bins in Gandan (50); Denjin (230), Hailast (100); Chingeltei (100); and
Dambadarjaa (1 15).
Project Standards and Criteria for Solid Waste
24.   To alleviate these problems, it was considered that under the project the criteria
should be that: the maximum distance that residents should be required to carry their
solid waste should be limited; and the locations of solid waste collection points needed to
be adjacent to suitable access roads, which in the cases of deeply ravined and floodplain
areas would be have to be paved if the service was to function in all weathers.
25.   From studies of the solid waste collection service, it also became apparent that
significant improvements to the service and costs could be recovered if larger collection
trucks were employed with compactor equipment. The project therefore aims to
supplement the pedestrian-access standard and introduce a vehicular access standard such
that:
(a)   No resident should need to carry their solid waste more than 200 m to an
authorized collection point.
(b)   All-weather roads would be proposed to facilitate collection by larger
collection vehicles.
(c)   The distances should be actual and not "as-the-crow-flies."
26.   A standard of 5.5 m for road pavement width was adopted to allow two-way
working of solid waste trucks and buses within the low trafficked areas.
27.   The width of 1.2 m for footpaths was adopted to allow handcarts to pass;
however, it is acknowledged that for much of the year it is possible to pass on the
adjacent surface and, therefore, the edge treatment of paths requires attention to detail.
Road and pedestrian bridges would be consistent with these standards.
Stormwater Drainage
28.   Ulaanbaatar is mainly drained by three rivers flowing from north to south into the
Tuul River, which runs east to west. These rivers are from east to west: Uliastai, Selbe,
Tolgoit and Naragiin.   Their tributaries include the Chingeltei, Hailast and



-146-                       ANNEX 15
Denjinmiyanga, which drain to the Selbe. Most of the drainage in the Ger area feeds into
the north-south rivers: Uliastai, Selbe, Tolgoit. Over the long term, the annual volume of
rainwater has been increasing and with it in recent years, flooding (in 1966, 1982 and
1994), a rise in the groundwater level and ground heave in expanding permafrost areas.
29.   A fundamental storm drainage problem is that Ger Khoroos have developed over
areas that are either steep-sloping or are adjacent to hills and are subject to seasonal storm
runoff. Ger areas also straddle the natural drainage routes to the river Tuul, as do formal
developments and the city center, which have been developed across the natural route of
the river Selbe.
30.   In addition, many of the tertiary channels have been filled with solid waste over
many years and while they may retain their drainage function, local flooding is caused
during the heaviest rainfall. On the other hand, in the absence of bridges, the deposits of
waste assist in providing access across the deeply ravined areas during the remainder of
the year.
31.   Another major problem is created by mudflows from the streams flowing to the
rivers Uliastai, Selbe, Tolgoit, and Naragiin. Mud and the larger shingle transported by
the main rivers themselves, restricts flow in the main drainage channels. However, no
regular attempt appears to be made to ensure that all the channels are clear and free of
solid waste. This is particularly so at culverts under roads that sometimes also suffer
from having less capacity than upstream channels.
32.   In the Denjin ger area, for example, there is are no formal stormwater drainage
provisions, while there are over 12 stormwater gullies and ravines with a total length of
9,000 m (lengths range from 120 m to 2,000 m). In winter, springwater charges freeze,
affecting 30-40 households. In summer, flooding results in human casualties and
property damage.
33.   The lack of maintenance of the piped stormwater system in the city is evident
during heavy storms as runoff is not accepted by the road gullies. Possibly many missing
gully covers were removed and traded as scrap metal; and many have been officially
removed and stored as a precaution against theft. All of this necessitates the regular
cleaning and emptying of each gully, for which there is no special equipment and,
consequently, the gullies are frequently full of debris.
34.   MUB had developed a program to alleviate flooding-in Ger and regular areas of
the city-as the drainage solutions are necessarily linked-and to reduce the rising
groundwater and permafrost expansion. These phenomena have been studied in detail by
MUB's Water Policy Unit (WPU). The WPU's main recommendation was to introduce a
system of horizontal and vertical ground drainage in Sukhbaatar (100 ail) to the north of
the city center.
35.   Following serious flooding in 1966, 1982 and 1994 in the north of the city, MUB
prepared an urgent program of works to prevent further damage. Without detailed



-147 -                     ANNEX 15
assessment it would be unwise to draw firm conclusions on the effectiveness of these
investments; however, inspection suggests that finding has sometimes dictated the extent
and dimensions of the sections used and these may be inadequate and inconsistent in
some locations.
36.  In Naran, for example, which is close to the city, an extensive cutoff drain was
built to protect Ger and the apartments below them, which can be densified-possibly
with apartments. But, the area above the channel and the apartments continues to develop
in Ger and is not protected from hillside runoff.
37.   Nevertheless, the incidences of serious flooding appear to have been reduced by
the works that have been completed and flooding continues to be concentrated in those
areas where projects have been identified but not yet implemented.
Selection Criteria for Drainage in Project Areas
38.   The following criteria were applied to select areas where project storm drainage
works would be implemented:
(a)   surveyed communities gave high priority to storm drainage improvements,
because of previous experience with damage due to flooding and erosion;
(b)   improvements to drainage capacity would improve and control runoff and
reduce the damage to property and lives; and
(c)   works previously identified MUB or the Water Policy Unit for inclusion in
their works program.
39.   Drainage proposals were estimated based on the experience of the existing
system. Two trapezoidal standards were adopted. Further detailed studies of the network
were made during project preparation in order to finalize cost estimates. Three standard
culverts were designed to allow uninterrupted flow through two standard channels and to
provide a standard solution for crossing the remaining natural ravines.
40.   The following stormwater drainage works would be carried out under the project
in three ger areas:
(a)   Denjin. Construction of both earth and concrete-faced channels and
filling of one eroded gully area and construction of a low-flow channel;
(b)   Chingeltei. Construction of an extension of the Chingeltei channel and
construction of piped drainage in badly scoured areas in the northeast
section of the Ger area; and
(c)   Dambadarjaa. Construction of flood protection walls and levees and
low-flow channels.



-148 -                    ANNEX 15
41.   A number of potentially worthwhile storm drainage projects were considered
during project preparation, but could not be included in the project because of constraints
on funding, implementation capacity and the need for a more methodical stormwater
drainage planning study. They are, however, worth mentioning, as outlined below with
preliminary 1995 cost estimates.
(a)   Diversion of the flow from the Denjinmiyanga River to the Selbe River
along the line of the channel recently constructed by the Municipality
(Tug 40 million).
(b)   Diversion of the Naragiin River north of the Oil Depot (and north of the
road through Tolgoit) (Tug 220 million).
(c)   Reinstatement of gully gratings in the city center with a hinged grating
(Tug 23 million).
(d)   Creation of a balancing lake on the Selbe River, north of Dambadarjaa.
(e)   Extension of the lake at Bayangol (Naran) with the raising of the dam
height.
(f)   Creation of a new balancing lake on the West Tolgoit River, north of
Songino-Khairkhan ( Baiyankoshuu).
(g)   Implementation of debris filters, whether as sabo dams or other devices on
the Selbe, Naragiin and Tolgoit Rivers.
(h)   Development of a storm drainage maintenance plan and provision of
maintenance equipment.
42.   Housing. In the UB Ger areas, housing is a mixture of many uninsulated, small
wooden buildings, a few brick or stucco buildings, with tarpaper roofs laid on boards, and
"Ger." The centuries-old Mongolian "Ger" is basically a cheap (about $600 in 1996)
one-room, circular (4-7 m diameter), mushroom-shaped, demountable tent, with
transportable wooden frames and a thick felt and canvas covering, with substantial
insulation but short heat retention value. Surveys indicate that in UB, permanent, heated
(but mostly uninsulated) wooden houses are replacing Ger. The Ger areas are being
transformed into private housing areas for all social groups, in the absence of funding
now and in the foreseeable future for apartment construction.
COST RECOVERY IN GER AREA UPGRADING
Introduction
43.   About $4.8 million (including contingencies on $3.2 million base costs) will be
spent under the project by MUB for infrastructure improvements including roads,
footpaths, stormwater drains, washing facilities and solid waste collections. The amount



-149 -                     ANNEX 15
does not include the cost of improvements under the project in Ger area water supply,
which will also be made in the areas to be upgraded.
44.   To establish priorities for improving Ger areas under the project, Ger areas were
ranked according to the following criteria:
(a)   health conditions and access to health services (e.g., socioeconomic
conditions);
(b)   population size and population density;
(c)   availability of services;
(d)   services construction and supply logistical conditions (e.g., ground
conditions, permafrost, slope, erosion and access, building relocation or
property acquisition requirements, which were given 50 percent weight in
the analysis);
(e)   town planning and future city development requirements; and
(f)   community willingness to participate in identification of priority
investments, and to pay ger improvement charges for them.
45.   About 86,000 people, or 16,900 households reside in the project Ger areas in
which infrastructure will be upgraded. These areas would also be benefited by the piped
water supply to be constructed under the project, which covers all 12 of the priority Ger
areas and would benefit about 169,000 people in 1,338 hectares of Ger area in 1996 or an
estimated 210,000 people in 2010.
46.   Due to resource constraints, only about 50 percent of the population in 12 Ger
areas suitable for improvement could be included in project; and only a minimum of
essential improvements could be made in those areas.  A substantial amount of
improvement work remains to be done in the future in both USIP and non-USIP areas,
when more resources can be made available.
47.   The objectives of a Ger Infrastructure Account (GIA) that would be established
under the project (see Operational Action Plan) are:
(a)   through GIA fees, to recover some of MUB's AusAID grant funding for
the Ger infrastructure improvement program;
(b)   to generate resources for infrastructure improvements in the Ger area,
which could not be included in the USIP because of constraints on
funding;
(c)   to charge GIA fees which are affordable; and



-150 -                      ANNEX 15
(d)   to encourage a sense of commitment among Ger residents to Ger
improvements.

maintenance of ger infrastructure, the establishment of a Ger Infrastructure Improvement
account was agreed to with MUB. Details of approaches to charges and generating
revenue for the account are given below and in the attached table.

-151-                      ANNEX 15
According to the Ger Infrastructure Fund Calculations:
(a)   Only ger households/plots getting project infrastructure ex-piped water
(86,400 people equivalent to about 17,000 ger plots) would be charged for
improvements;
(b)   An affordable charge of 1 percent of average household income would be
charged;
(c)   Ger beneficiaries would be charged only for on-site improvements,
defined as being roads, paths, storm drains, etc., which are located in the
immediate vicinity of Ger plots and primarily benefit adjacent ger plots.
The rest of the road and stormwater drainage improvements would be
considered as off-site improvements, which link ger area to the central city
system and benefit the city population as a whole, and costs would be
recovered from general city revenues, including possibly a future property
tax.
(d)   The charges would be collected quarterly from ger plot holders by the
Khoroos, in the same manner as solid waste charges are now collected;
and would commence in each Ger area as and when improvements were
completed in an area. The charges would be revised annually by MUB to
correspond to the inflationary increase in improvement costs, except that
the percentage increase in the charge would not exceed the percentage
increase in ger incomes in the previous year.
(e)   MUB would establish a separate Ger Improvement Account maintained as
a Revolving Fund in a separate account;
(f)   Collections of improvement charges accruing to the Account would be
used only for additional improvements in project ger areas, nonproject Ger
areas, ger infrastructure maintenance, or such other Ger area activities as
might be agreed between IDA and MUB from time to time.
(g)   As illustrated in table below, a charge of Tug 500/month/plot, equivalent
to about 1 percent of average 1996 ger household income, would generate
about Tug 102 million per year ($156,000 equivalent) and recover 50
percent of the $2.5 million (base cost) of ger improvements in eight years.
Benefits
49.   The Benefits of the user charge, established as described above, and illustrated in
the table attached would be:
(a)   A modest recovery of funds invested by MUB in Ger infrastructure
improvements;



-152 -                     ANNEX 15
(b)   Affordable Improvement Charges. The charges, set at a small percentages
of Ger household income, would be affordable to Ger households who are
the poorest segment of the UB population;
(c)   Additional Ger Infrastructure Improvements. The revenues in the ger area
improvement account could be used by MUB to implement small high-
priority infrastructure improvements in project and nonproject Ger area,
which could not be improved under USIP; and
(d)   Enhancement of the market value of Ger plots, particularly in view of the
anticipated privatization of Ger land and strengthening of tenure on ger
plots, which the payment of the infrastructure charge would also
strengthen.
